==PAGE:1==
   
 
 
IMPLEMENTATION OF FINITE ELEMENT ANALYSIS IN THE 
PARAMETRIC DESIGN OF A SHELL STRUCTURE WITH OPENINGS  
MASTER THESIS OF TINA MOIKJÆR HARKSEN 
 
 
 
 
 
 
M.Sc. Architectural Engineering 
Kgs. Lyngby 
July 2016 
 
==PAGE:2==
 
 
==PAGE:3==
In this thesis, the Finite Element program Karamba has been studied for the application of 
structural analysis in a parametric design process. The study has its focal point in a shell structure 
with openings, which has set the requirements for the analyses. The motivation lies within the 
time consuming and inflexible nature of other available Finite Element programs, and the ambition 
is to improve the potential of using Finite Element Analysis as a tool for structural design. Karamba 
is a plugin for Grasshopper¸ a visual programming tool, which is typically used for parametric 
modelling. The advantage of applying a Finite Element program, which is incorporated in a 
parametric modelling tool, is that it eliminates the need for changing model format, and reduces 
the time spent on setting up Finite Element models. The goal of applying Karamba is to make the 
design process more efficient and informed, by analysing more designs in shorter time. 
Karamba has been tested two ways: By breaking down the necessary analyses to their basis, and 
testing these on analytical models, and by comparing the analyses of the full shell model to another 
Finite Element program, Robot Structural Analysis. The applicability of Karamba in the concerned 
design case has been evaluated by assessing the types of design decisions, which can be made 
based on the quality of the analyses. The design decisions have then been mapped in the design 
process using Level of Development. 
Karamba has been found successful in the basic abilities of shell analysis, membrane behaviour, 
bending behaviour and linear buckling, when tested on the analytical models. When analysing the 
specific shell structure with openings, it has been found that the meshing of the shell, is crucial 
for the accuracy of the results. 
From the accuracy levels achieved, it has been deduced that Karamba, within reason, can be used 
for comparing design variations on forces, deformations and buckling stiffness and for 
approximate dimensioning of the cross section with respect to stresses. Furthermore, the flexibility 
of working in a parametric design environment has been found helpful in creating a Finite Element 
model for a shell structure.

1  INTRODUCTION 
1 
 
1.1 THESIS STATEMENT 
This thesis searches to propose a solution for tying together the geometrical model and the model 
for structural analysis in a parametric design process. The main object is to find a solution, which 
eliminates the need for a change in model format. With solving this problem comes the potential 
of: 
 
Reducing the time spent on performing structural analysis 
 
Improving the quality of the design with more design iterations 
 
Reducing the price of the structure due to quality improvement and early 
detection of structural complications 
 
Making structural analysis more accessible to non-experts  
By reducing the amount of manual work needed for a structural engineer to perform Finite 
Element Analysis the intent is to improve the design process in such a way that it not only leads 
to time savings, but ultimately to more well-informed structural design.  
1.2 MOTIVATION 
The motivation for this thesis stems from the aspiration of combining parametric and structural 
analysis, such that easily interchangeable building models can be used not only for exploring 
design variations from an aesthetical viewpoint, but also from a structural viewpoint. In the world 
of architectural engineering, it is common knowledge that introducing structural consideration 
early in a design process, will help improve the economy and the quality of the design. 
For repetitive beam-column structures, quick hand calculations and look-ups of standard elements 
can ensure a realistic design far along in the design process. For shell structures however, 
analytical models only apply to very general cases and aside from those, hand calculations are 
virtually impossible to carry out. The more detailed the design becomes, the harder it gets for the 
structural engineer to draw on personal experience, because what is the beauty of shell structures 
is also the challenge: the design options are endless and unforeseeable. Because of the diversity 
of shell structures, they are often designed in a geometrically driven, parametric design 
environment. 
1 INTRODUCTION
==PAGE:12==
 
1  INTRODUCTION 
2 
 
The most sensible way to analyse a non-generic shell structure is to perform computerised Finite 
Element Analysis, FEA. With most of the commonly used Finite Element software, this would mean 
a change in model format, and setting up a Finite Element model for each design change, which 
can be quite a comprehensive step, when the basis is a purely geometrical model. With traditional 
Finite Element modelling being very time consuming, FEA has the risk of being put off until the 
design is fairly fixed. This leaves a gap for quick structural analysis, which can be performed with 
each change in the design, leading to well-informed design.  
Karamba is a program, which facilitates the incorporation of structural analysis into a parametric 
design process. However, through personal experience and research of other people’s use of the 
program, it is found that Karamba is used as an estimative design tool, more than as a tool for 
detailed FEA. Since it is build up as a Finite Element program, it seems natural, to investigate the 
potential of the program, for more extensive and detailed analysis, which has led to a study of the 
analysis options and accuracy of Karamba, and ultimately how this can be applied in the design 
process of a shell structure.  
In shell design, a large emphasis is put on optimal structures, since these are the essence of what 
shell theory teaches us. Despite that, the everyday design situation is usually much more practical. 
Often a design is initiated with a suggestion from the architect, and the design will then be changed 
and perfected with respect to an array of considerations, such as use, structural performance, 
energy performance, execution etc. 
The situation aimed to improve with this study, is the dialogue between the architect and the 
engineer, which leads them from initial design to finished geometry. The goal is for the engineer 
to implement a change, and receive an immediate feedback of how the change affects e.g. the 
maximum stress, the stress in a specific area or the deformations. The results will be determining 
for the cross section of the shell, which will then go on to influence the price, the environmental 
imprint, the appearance etc. This would help to inform the discussion of the quality of competing 
design suggestions and ideally open of for solutions, which are not already well-tested.  
 
 
==PAGE:13==
 
1  INTRODUCTION 
3 
 
1.3 CASE STUDY 
A specific point of departure is taken in a concrete shell structure with significant openings. The 
shell is a part of the new KUA University Square designed by COBE Architects, and is going to 
create shelter over a bike parking area as a hill within the pavement. The shell is particularly 
interesting for this study, because the behaviour of a shell with openings is even harder to predict 
than that of a continuous shell, which means that the benefits of performing structural analysis 
throughout the design process are also greater. The case study serves the purpose of representing 
a design situation, where the architect proposes a design, which is based on some structural 
consultancy but no structural analysis. Considering the design as an early proposal, changes can 
arise due to structural findings or new demands to the functionality of the shell 
 
Figure 1.1 ‒ Shell structure for bike parking at KUA University Square by COBE Architects 
Three shell structures will be build in the same area, but only the largest will be included in this 
study. The shells are all made up of spherical caps with large cut outs, which are circular in the 
plan. A plan and a section of the largest shell can be found in Figure 1.2. The shell will be denoted 
ø50 in the following, due to the base diameter of the spherical cap.   
It is assumed that the shell will be build as a in-situ cast reinforced concrete shell. There is no easy 
way of constructing shell structures, so for this study it is chosen to focus on the smooth, 
continuous shell which comes with in-situ casting. The smooth shell is particularly interesting in 
assessing the quality of a Finite Element Model of plane elements. In-situ casting comes with a 
number of complications such as imperfections, shrinkage and environmental impacts, which will 
only briefly be discussed in this report. Also, concrete will be considered as linear elastic 
throughout this study. 
In this study only Karamba’s abilities regarding shell behaviour will be tested, which means that 
the foundations will not be included. The foundations are a major consideration in the structure, 
especially due to the amount of horisontal forces which comes with the shallowness of the shell. 
By creating a reinforced foundation ring, horisontal forces can be carried as tension in the ring. 
==PAGE:14==
 
1  INTRODUCTION 
4 
 
The foundation could furthermore be supplied with pre-tension, in order to neutralise 
deformations from shrinkage, creep and self-weight. The foundation ring has been excluded from 
this study, since the load transfer from shell to foundation is very hard to model realisticly in a 
Finite Element model, and could easily make up an entire study on its own. 
 
 
Figure 1.2 ‒ Plan and section of ø50 shell 
 
 
==PAGE:15==
 
1  INTRODUCTION 
5 
 
1.4 METHODOLOGY 
The objective of studying the applicability of Karamba in a design process, stems from the case 
study, where there through discussion with CN3 has been registered an insufficiency in the 
options for structural analysis, which can be integrated in the design dialogue with the architect. 
Through a literature study of the process of building design, the current use of Finite Element 
Modeling and the interoporatibility of FEM-tools with other programs, it was recognised that a 
fairly large gap in the design process exists in the field of structural analysis. This led to the open 
ended question: How far in the design process can Karamba be used to make informed decisions?  
A literature study of shell theory and the experience of structural designers in designing shells has 
been carried out, in order to determine the analyses necessary and what can be expected of the 
behaviour of a concrete shell with openings. 
The actual testing of Karamba is separated into two sections. In the first part of the testing, the 
analysis types in question are tested on structures with simple, analytical solutions. Analytical 
meaning that they can be calculated by hand. Testing different structural behaviour separately, 
gives a good indication of the quality of the different computational methods, and potentially 
pinpoints the origin of any major deviations. 
In the second part of the testing, Karamba is tested on the shell with openings, where there are no 
analytical solutions. Another Finite Element program, namely Autodesk’s Robot Structural Analysis 
is used for comparison of the accuracy of the results. For this assessment, it is important to note 
that no absolute conclusions can be made on the accuracy of the Karamba results, only on the 
performance compared to the performance of Robot. It is not the scope of the study to test Robot, 
it is merely chosen as a representative of the more trustworthy but time-consuming Finite Element 
programs.  
As the tests have been customised to a specific structure, conclusions can only be made regarding 
this specific shell with openings. However, by basing the assessment on an array of general, 
methodical test, it is made likely, that results of other types of shells will be of a similar nature. 
In assessing the extent of the application of Karamba, Level of Development will be used as a 
measure. With the knowledge of what analyses can be performed and the accuracy level of the 
results, an assessment is made on what kind of design decisions can be made based on Karamba, 
and hence to what level the design can be developed, before a change in Finite Element program 
is necessary.  
==PAGE:16==
 
1  INTRODUCTION 
6 
 
1.5 OUTLINE 
This report can be separated into three main parts: Theory, testing and application. 
The theory part consists of two sections, which serve the purpose of giving the reader a basic 
understanding of the terms used throughout the testing and assessment of Karamba. The first is 
a Background section, where an introduction is made to the applied software and the angle on 
building design process used to shape the study. The second section introduces Fejl! 
Henvisningskilde ikke fundet., which covers shell theory in terms of membrane action, shell 
bending and buckling. 
The testing part also consists of two sections, the first of which is a set of Methodical Tests, where 
all the Karamba analysis types relevant for the structure are tested against analytical models. All 
the methodical tests will be initiated with a brief introduction to the Karamba component and the 
theory of the analytical model. The second part of the testing is a Comparison of Finite Element 
Software, where the analyses are carried out on the shell with and without the openings, and the 
results are compared to those of corresponding Robot Structural Analysis models. The analyses of 
this section will be carried out with a number of different meshes, as these are particularly decisive 
for the results in the case of a shell with openings. 
In the application part of the report, Karamba in the Design Process, the test results will be 
summarised to evaluate the substantiated application of Karamba. A review will also be made of 
the practical benefits of performing Finite Element Analysis with Karamba within the Grasshopper 
environment. Three small design studies are carried out, to exemplify design changes, to which 
Karamba can be applied.  
 
==PAGE:17==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
7 
 
2.1 TOOLS 
2.1.1 FINITE ELEMENT ANALYSIS 
A brief introduction to the Finite Element Method will be given here. The mathematics behind FEM 
will only be touched on, where necessary for the understanding of the results.  
The Finite Element Method, FEM, is a numerical method created for performing structural analysis 
on structures without an analytical solution. The method subdivides continuous structures into a 
collection of small elements. These elements are connected to each other in nodes. By evaluating 
the structure only at the nodes, an approximation to the result of the continuous structure can be 
found. With a finite number of elements with shared nodes, a set of equilibrium equations of the 
system can be set up and solved, in order to determine the displacement in each node. The set of 
equilibrium equations can be written in Matrix form as: 
K is called the stiffness matrix and it holds information of the cross section and material stiffness. 
R is a vector of the load in each node. D is a vector of the unknown displacements, for which the 
system is solved. The displacement field, D, can then be used to determine stresses in the nodes 
as well. 
Most of the mathematics in the Finite Element Method lies within the elements. The element type 
determines what kind of deformations can be developed in the element. Sometimes it is necessary 
to perform several analyses on a structure with different element types, since one element type 
does not cover all the behaviours of the structure. Surface elements range from planar, triangular 
3-node elements to curved, quadrilateral 9-node elements, and new element types are constantly 
under development, in order to suit specific problem.   
The stiffness matrix is first set up on an element basis, meaning that the stiffness matrix is not 
only dependent on the material and cross section, but also of the element type. The element 
stiffness matrices are then put together to form one matrix of the entire structure, such that each 
node is influenced by all the elements connecting in that node. The system of equations easily 
becomes very large, which is why Finite Element Analysis, FEA, is usually carried out with 
computer force.  
2 BACKGROUND
 
R
D
K


( 2-1 ) 
==PAGE:18==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
8 
 
Within the nodes of the elements is a certain number of Degrees of Freedom, DOFs, which control 
the types of deformation allowed in the node. The possible DOFs consist of a translation and a 
rotation in each of the three directions of a local coordinate system, but in the simplest cases, only 
one DOF is needed. The DOFs do not only control the deformations, but also the options for loads 
and supports.  
The application of different element types will not be covered in this study, as Karamba only has 
one type of shell elements. This is a three node triangular element with 6 degrees of freedom in 
each node, as illustrated in Figure 2.1. The most important thing to take away from the element 
nodes having 6 DOFs, is that it can be used for both in-plane and bending behaviour. The elements 
do not have any in plane rotational stiffness, which is a common assumption for shell elements. 
 
Figure 2.1 ‒ DOFs in Karamba shell element 
The division of structures into smaller segments is called meshing. The meshing is critical for the 
accuracy of the results, generally the finer the mesh, the more accurate the result. On the other 
hand the finer the mesh is, the larger the equation system becomes, which places demands on 
computational time and power. Reducing the size of the elements is often mentioned as mesh 
refinement and is especially important in areas, where there is a drastic change in stresses, since 
the structure is only evaluated at the nodes of the elements.   
Since Finite Element Analysis provides an approximation to the actual results, convergence 
analysis is an important tool for determining an appropriate mesh refinement for the structure. 
By performing the same analysis with an increasing number of elements, it can be checked 
whether a specific result converges towards a certain value, as illustrated in Figure 2.2, or the 
results are inconsistent. The ideal situation for a convergence analysis is that the results stabilises 
nicely with the increase of the number of elements, which means that a mesh refinement can be 
chosen and the results will be approximately the same as if double or ten times the amount of 
elements were used.  
==PAGE:19==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
9 
 
 
Figure 2.2 ‒ Example of convergence analysis 
In most cases, stress analysis will be carried out solely on the non-deformed structure, i.e. a linear 
analysis. These are the kind of calculations we know from basic mechanics, where the 
deformations and section forces are determined from the original geometry. Linear analysis is a 
simplification of the actual behaviour of a structure, but when the deformations are relatively 
small, it is a very reasonable simplification. 
In some cases, where the deformations are large enough, that they give rise to a significant change 
in the force distribution, a geometrically non-linear analysis can be necessary. The geometrically 
non-linear analysis will be referred to as non-linear analysis throughout the report. The main 
concept of the non-linear analysis is, to apply the load in increments, and update the geometry to 
the deformed shape after each load increment. In Figure 2.3 two examples are shown of non-linear 
analyses, one with simple load increments, and one where iterations are made to find new 
equilibrium in the deformed system. The black curve represents the theoretically non-linear 
solution, and the blue represents the non-linear analyses. Non-linear analyses are computationally 
heavy, since linear analysis has to be carried out for each load increment, and even more so, when 
each increment is subdivided into iteration steps. Non-linear analysis is therefore mostly reserved 
for very flexible structures and buckling behaviour, where drastic deformations occur. 
==PAGE:20==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
10 
 
 
Figure 2.3 ‒ Examples of non-linear load/displacement curves 
2.1.2 PARAMETRIC DESIGN 
Parametric design pivots around a model, which is set up with a number of chosen variables, such 
that the design can be modified in a specific manner, by changing the variables. The parametric 
model is set up by creating a specific algorithmic relationship between the variables, either directly 
through scripting or indirectly with the help of a parametric modelling tool.   
The strength and the restriction of parametric design is that the parameters must be chosen 
consciously; hence, all outcomes can in theory be predicted, as combinations of different 
parameter settings. With a set of consciously chosen parameters, the parametric model has a great 
advantage on traditional 3d-models, since an endless number of design variations can be made, 
without having to remodel.   
If parametric modelling is used from a very early design stage, it might be necessary to redo the 
model, when big decisions have been made, as the same parameters are not important throughout 
the design process. In the first stages, the design will be parametrised with respect to overall 
volumetric aspects, then the geometry will have to be detailed, and as the design progresses, it is 
often relevant to vary the properties of the material and the cross section.  
Object based parametric modelling, is a type of parametric modelling, where information of cross 
section, material, appearance etc. are tied to geometrical elements such as lines or faces. The 
relationship between the objects in the model make up the building model. This type of modelling 
is the basis of Building Information Modelling, where the intent is to have one model holding all 
the information of a building. The options for geometric variation in object based parametric 
modelling is often limited to fairly regular operations, which is why there is a use for geometrical 
parametric modelling, where only the geometry is the primary focus.   
==PAGE:21==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
11 
 
Grasshopper is a visual programming tool, which is made as a plugin for the 3d-modelling software 
Rhinoceros, especially for geometrical, parametric modelling, both of which are programs of 
Robert McNeel & Associates. Grasshopper is a great tool for parametric design, since it gives 
access to the functions behind the geometry, which can be build up as a script, with the desired 
relations to create complex geometries. Very little to no experience with programming is needed 
to use Grasshopper, as it uses a highly intuitive system of functions. These are connected with 
cables, to form the script, and example of which is shown in Figure 2.4, with the generation of a 
hemispherical surface.  
Where transformation in traditional Computer Aided Design, CAD, has been restricted to the basic 
concepts of translation, scaling and rotation, Grasshopper gives a much larger freedom in 
specifying exactly what and how to transform. Furthermore, the geometrical functions can be 
interchanged and the geometry will automatically be rebuild. 
        
 
Figure 2.4 ‒ Grasshopper generation of hemisphere 
Shell structures are very often designed within a geometry based, parametric environment. This 
can partially be explained by design variations of doubly curved shells being much less intuitive 
than the variations of e.g. regular beam-column structures are. In addition, much more 
geometrically complex operations are needed in order to shape shells of freeform nature. 
Grasshopper is specifically useful for shell design, as it is a platform for plugins, which can perform 
form-finding and optimisation procedures.   
Grasshopper has a long list of expansions, where some expand the geometrical functions, some 
facilitate collaboration with other programs and others can be used for e.g. structural or daylight 
analysis.  
2.1.3 KARAMBA 
Karamba is a Finite Element program made as an expansion for Grasshopper. The first version 
only targeted one-dimensional structural members, i.e. beams and rods, but in the recent versions 
shell elements have become available as well. The program was created for performing form-
finding and optimisation routines with a direct connection to the geometrical model. 
Parameter 
Surface 
==PAGE:22==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
12 
 
The creator of Karamba, Clemens Preisinger, says about the intent of Karamba: 
Karamba ‒ It is easy to use for non-experts, has been tailored to the needs of architects 
and engineers in the early design phase, works interactively and costs slightly less than 
the rest. (Preisinger, 2015) 
A Karamba model is build up from components as any other Grasshopper model. A Karamba shell 
model takes in the geometry as a surface mesh, and with the addition of supports, loads, cross 
section and material, a model can be assembled as illustrated in Figure 2.5. Different types of 
analyses can then be run on this model, and a variety of results can be extracted.  
 
Figure 2.5 - Assembly of Karamba shell model 
Karamba has an advantage over traditional FEM-programs, since it does not only hold a variety of 
pre-sets for loads, support, materials and cross sections, it also has a great freedom in creating 
specialised features, within the parametric environment of Grasshopper.  
Although Karamba has a much simpler interface than many traditional FEM-programs once you 
are familiar with the Grasshopper logic, it is important to stress one of the cornerstones in FEA: ‘if 
you put nonsense in, you will get nonsense out’. This meaning that you have to have an 
understanding of the information which is put into the analysis in order to get meaningful results. 
The intent of Karamba itself leads to the purpose of this study: Can it be used for more than just 
the early, conceptual stages of the design process?  
==PAGE:23==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
13 
 
2.1.4 ROBOT 
Robot Structural Analysis is a Finite Element program developed by Autodesk. Robot is a quite 
established program for structural analysis, especially since it is an Autodesk product, which 
means that it is intended for interoperability with other Autodesk software, such as Revit and 
AutoCad. Robot is not the FE program with the most options for complex analysis; however, it has 
the advantage of having a good user interface, which is not always the case for FE program, as 
they sometimes rely mostly on coding. It has been the goal to model the shell directly in the chosen 
FE program, in order to eliminate errors from export and import. Robot being an Autodesk product 
means that the user interface includes a decent amount of options for modelling of the geometry, 
which is a rarity in Finite Element modelling.  
Robot is chosen as a representative of the category Established Finite Element programs, not 
because it is necessarily more accurate, but because it more user-friendly and better suited for 
shell structures than most FE programs.  
2.2 DESIGN PROCESS  
Design process is a term, which can be interpreted in many ways, but in this case, the phase model 
according to FRI and DanskeArk (2012), is used to give an understanding of the process from 
demand to finished building. The phase model consists of five phases, two of which can be 
subdivided in order to describe the design process more thoroughly, as illustrated in Figure 2.6. 
 
Figure 2.6 - Phase model 
The initial phase is called the programming phase, this is where all the fundamental questions 
are raised, what do we have, what do we need, what are the resources, what are the expectations, 
which will be the core of the design. In the building industry, there are often multiple actors 
==PAGE:24==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
14 
 
involved already at this stage. The programming phase is very schematic, and involves hardly any 
spatial considerations. 
The outline proposal is where the spatial design is initiated, and the architectural concept, main 
materials and structural principal must be determined. The design of the outline proposal is of a 
conceptual order and an array of different geometries are held up against the design criteria set 
up in the programming phase. The outline proposal is expected to hold a couple of coarse drawings 
illustrating the size and the expression of the structure. 
The second part of the proposal phase is the project proposal, where one specific design proposal 
is chosen. The proposal is validated and communicated through semi-coarse drawings and 
estimative calculations on structure and economy. 
The early design phases have come under heavy spotlight in modern times, especially when 
considering the Macleamy Curve, Figure 2.7, which is often used to illustrate the intent of Building 
Information Modelling. The Macleamy curve tells us that in the ideal design process, much more 
effort is put into the early phases, since this is where large changes can be made, with little effort 
and cost. The early stages are commonly mentioned as the conceptual design phases. In the BIM 
Handbook the concept of the Macleamy curve is substantiated with: 
Conceptual design ‒ It anticipates and considers the full range of expertise of the 
design team. (Eastman, et al., 2011) 
This is a very essential part of why conceptual design should be prioritised. The expertise of the 
different actors is utilised to predict and solve problems before they occur.  
 
Figure 2.7- Macleamy curve [Figure from Eastman et al., 2011] 
==PAGE:25==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
15 
 
The preliminary project is also called the regulatory project, since this is the stage, at which the 
first authorisation is sought. The preliminary project must convey that all local regulations are met 
and that the structure can realistically be build. The main aspects of the authorisation has to do 
with plot ratio, height and appearance, meaning that the approximate size and choice of material 
must have been established. 
The main project is the phase where the design is developed into a fully functional and 
constructible structure. Technical details are designed to make sure that the structure lives up to 
all technical requirements, with respect to strength, indoor climate, energy consumption etc. This 
means that analysis is a major part of this stage. Performing analysis on a building will often mean 
a change in model format, some more extensive than others, depending on the interoperability 
between the modelling and the analysis software. The main project phase rounds of the actual 
design process. 
The execution phase is where the material of the main project is put to the test. Poorly planned 
projects can lead to massive extra expenses in the execution phase, since small mistakes can lead 
to major errors. 
The operation phase covers the entire lifetime of the building and shows whether the building 
performs as expected.  
2.2.1 STRUCTURAL ANALYSIS IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
Working with shell structures, the obvious choice for structural analysis is to use a Finite Element 
program, since hand calculations of such can be very comprehensive and would often necessitate 
a great amount of simplifications of the model for calculation.  
When using Grasshopper for parametric modelling of the geometry, the model of the shell has to 
be translated into data, which is importable for the chosen FEM-software, in this case Autodesk 
Robot is the reference. There are currently a number of options for how to approach the 
interoperability between Grasshopper and Robot. The simplest of which, is to transform the 
geometry into a basic geometric format i.e. .SAT, which can be imported in Robot, and then the 
FE-model must be set up manually. A more efficient solution is to use Geometry Gym, which is a 
plugin for Grasshopper that includes functions for setting up a Robot model within the 
Grasshopper environment. However, Geometry Gym is still under development and the options 
are not very well-documented.  
Though changing between specialised software is appropriate for extremely detailed and 
comprehensive analyses, it is a hurdle in a dynamic, parametric design process. Even though the 
interoperability between building modelling software is evolving rapidly, there is still a need for 
manual operations and a risk of data loss, when shifting between file formats. In addition, getting 
==PAGE:26==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
16 
 
an immediate response to a design change, gives a much better understanding of the structure, 
and the coherence between different design variations. 
The extensive process towards a traditional Finite Element model yields that FEA is mainly used 
in the last part of the planning phase, for determination of cross sectional dimensions and material 
strength. Once the geometry is fixed, it takes very little effort to change the thickness or the 
material properties within traditional FE programs. 
Karamba on the other hand is created as a form-finding tool, i.e. to find the optimal shape from 
for example a boundary curve, a mesh and a load. Karamba is therefore meant to be used at a very 
early stage, to create a geometry, which is optimal for a certain load type. Optimal structures, 
though highly satisfying from a structural point of view, are a rarity in reality, both because of 
design demands from the architect and because they are not as optimal in a real life application. 
This means that not only is the intended use of Karamba limited in how far in the design process 
it applies, it is also limited in the amount of design cases it applies to. 
Karamba and traditional FE software take up either end of the design process, as illustrated in 
Figure 2.8a. In the phases between, the process is largely driven by experience from other projects, 
and when highly necessary, an inefficient FE model is created. The lines are drawn very harshly, 
where in reality designing is not as segmented as it has been illustrated here, and every process 
is different. 
The purpose of this study is to investigate an expansion of the use of Karamba, such that it covers 
as large a part of the structural analysis as possible. In other words, as many design decisions as 
possible, should be made based on the Karamba results. Furthermore, it is intended to implement 
Karamba in a more practical design case, where a design is proposed by the architect, and changes 
can be motivated by i.e. aesthetics, economy or functionality. The potential role of Karamba, which 
is the aim of the study, is shown in Figure 2.8b.  
The goal is not to replace traditional Finite Element Analysis, FEA, but rather to make sure, that 
the structural considerations are included in every step of the design process, in a way that is 
timesaving and automate, and has great freedom with respect to modelling and post-processing. 
By expanding the use of Karamba, the need for traditional FEA, necessitating a change in model 
format, is greatly reduced. Ideally, it will not be necessary to use a more comprehensive FE-tool 
before the geometry is determined. 
==PAGE:27==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
17 
 
 
(a) Current role 
(b) Object of study 
Figure 2.8 ‒ Role of Karamba within the phase model 
2.2.2 LEVEL OF DEVELOPMENT 
The phase model is an outline for what material should be finished at what point, and is usually 
adapted and detailed for the particular building case. However, in terms of mapping design 
decisions in this project, the scale has been found to be too fluid and general. This is where Level 
of Development is introduced, because it already holds structure-specific restrictions. 
Level of Development, LOD, is a concept of Building Information Modelling, which categorises the 
extent to which model elements have been developed, i.e. how much thought through detail is 
included in the model element. LOD is an ambiguous subject, so in this case the Level of 
Development Specification (BIMForum, 2013) is used for reference. Some also use LOD to describe 
Level of Detail, which is merely a measurement of the appearance of the element, in the form of 
the detail included (BIMForum, 2013).  
LOD is a helpful way of categorising the development of the design, since in reality designing, i.e. 
as depicted in Figure 2.8, is not a strictly chronological process. The design process involves a lot 
of looping and jumping back and forth, and not all parts of the structure will be developed 
simultaneously. The LOD will then communicate which design aspects have been determined, and 
which are still unsettled.   
In this case, LOD will not only be used to describe to development of the model elements, but the 
development of the design in general. This is a twist on the purpose of LOD, but since this study 
only deals with the structural system, it is justifiable, because the shell structure is one unit. As 
the structure is considered as one element, the LOD will mainly serve as a more tangible scale for 
categorising the development, which a design decision represents.  
==PAGE:28==
 
2  BACKGROUND 
18 
 
The Level of Development Specification has detailed descriptions of the LOD categories, 
customised to different kind of building elements. As shells are not a part of the original 
specification, a combination of the concrete deck and concrete wall specifications is made to fit a 
shell with openings.  
An interpretation of the LODs, where structural design is included, is given below. The LODs are 
described in terms of what needs to be defined for the specific Level of Development, and how to 
come to these decisions. The LODs can be subdivided for more precision, i.e. LOD250 between 
LOD200 and LOD300. Higher levels exists, but by LOD400 the full structural design and 
dimensions should be established.

==PAGE:30==
 
3  THEORY OF SHELL STRUCTURES 
20 
 
The simplest definition of a shell structure is a three dimensional structure, where two dimensions 
are significantly larger than the third. This means that shell structure can be described by a surface 
with a thickness, the simplest of which is a plate. For the sake of clarity, a distinction will be made 
between plates, described by plane surfaces, and shells, described by curved surfaces. The surface 
describing the shell will typically be the mid-surface, meaning that the thickness is distributed 
equally on each side of the surface. 
The reason why shell structures are separated from plates is that shells have the ability to carry 
transversal loads as in-plane forces as illustrated in Figure 3.1, whereas plates carry transversal 
forces through pure bending. The curvature of a shell structure provides great stiffness, whereas 
plates solely rely on the cross section for bending stiffness. The optimal condition for a shell is 
when no bending occurs ‒ these are called membrane shells. 
              
Figure 3.1- Plate and shell with transversal load 
Another distinction will be given to the term shell structure: They must be primarily in 
compression. This separates the shell structures from membranes, which are based on the same 
shell theory, but in practise, it seems logical to have a distinction between the compressed shell 
structures such as concrete domes and masonry vaults, and the tensioned membranes such as 
tents and sail structures.  
Stone based materials have always been preferred for construction of shell structures, because 
carrying transverse loads through compression is a great utilisation of materials of great 
compression strength and little tensile strength. With modern methods for analysis it has become 
apparent that shell structures can be build much thinner than e.g. traditional stone and masonry 
shells. The evolution of shell thickness and more irregular shell geometries means that concrete 
is an obvious choice of material, because it is so easily adaptable. When the shells become very 
thin, new complications arise, which is why reinforcement is needed. Amongst the primary reasons 
for adding reinforcement, it can be mentioned that: 
3 THEORY OF SHELL STRUCTURES
==PAGE:31==
 
3  THEORY OF SHELL STRUCTURES 
21 
 
 
The shells are more sensitive to uneven loads, because the self-weight is greatly reduced 
in thin shells. 
 
Small bending moments can lead to tension, because the compression forces are greatly 
reduced in thin shells. 
 
Thin shells are prone to buckling, which calls for bending stiffness. 
Additionally reinforcement help to manage the stresses occurring from creep and shrinkage. 
As mentioned, most shell structures will have both membrane and bending behaviour, as shown 
in Figure 3.2, which will be introduced in the following. Most shell theories neglect transverse 
shear deformations, which is a reasonable assumption when working with thin shells. 
            
 
Figure 3.2 ‒ Shell section forces 
3.1 MEMBRANE THEORY 
Membrane theory covers the concept of carrying in loads through in-plane forces, also called 
membrane forces. The membrane forces are parallel to the tangent plane of the mid-surface in 
each point, and they consist of a normal force, Nx and Ny, and a shear force, Nxy and Nyx, in each 
direction as illustrated on a small cut out of a shell in Figure 3.3. The membrane forces are constant 
over the thickness of the shell.  
 
Figure 3.3 - Membrane forces in shell 
From equilibrium considerations, it can be found that for homogenous shells the two in plane 
shear forces are of the same size: 
 
yx
xy
N
N

 
( 3-1 )  
==PAGE:32==
 
3  THEORY OF SHELL STRUCTURES 
22 
 
In most cases, the extreme membrane forces will be of greatest interest, these are called the 
principal membrane forces. The principal membrane forces N1 and N2 make up a set of orthogonal 
forces, which can be determined from any other set of orthogonal forces, e.g. Nx and Ny, as seen 
in ( 3-2 ). A useful characteristic of the principal membrane forces is that in the coordinate system 
of the principal membrane forces, the in-plane shear force is 0, i.e. N12=0. This is helpful when the 
cross section is dimensioned, since the maximum and minimum membrane forces can be 
considered whilst neglecting the in plane shear. 
In order to achieve pure membrane forces, a number of conditions have to be fulfilled, some of 
which are: 
 
The shell has to be thin enough that the bending stiffness is insignificant 
 
The surface must be smooth and continuous 
 
Supports must be tangential to the mid-surface 
 
Loads should be distributed, not concentrated 
Violation of any of these rules will cause bending of the shell, but for local violation, the bending 
will also be local, and not affect the entire shell. 
Pure membrane forces can only be achieved under very specific loading and support conditions, 
which are almost impossible to maintain in reality. A theoretically perfect membrane shell is 
designed for one particular load case, typically dead load, which will only be the reality, if the 
structure is protected against all wind, snow and live load. Even in that case, the construction will 
introduce imperfections to the shell. For this reason, shells that are designed to have very little 
bending will often be called membrane shells in practice. 
3.2 SHELL BENDING 
Although shells work most efficiently, when they are free of bending, it cannot be avoided 
altogether. Simple supports will have some resistance, asymmetric forces will occur and zero 
bending stiffness is just a simplification. Some types of bending behaviour can be analysed and 
others must be accepted as simplifications.  
The shell bending forces consist of a bending moment, Mx and My, a torsional moment, Mxy and 
Myx, and an out of plane shear force, Qx and Qy, for each section, as illustrated in Figure 3.4.

Shell bending will always be combined with membrane forces, which leads to a statically 
indeterminate system. This means that shell bending cannot be solved analytically, and numerical 
methods such as the Finite Element Method must be used. 
The maximum and minimum moments, i.e. the principal moments, can be found in the same 
manner as for the membrane forces: 
Similarly, the torsional moments are zero in the direction of the principal moments. It should be 
noted that the direction of the principal membrane forces and the principal moments are 
independent of each other. 
As bending in shells is a complicated matter, bending theories are typically subjects of 
simplifications. The bending theory used in the Karamba shell elements is the Kirchhoff-Love 
hypothesis. This theory assumes that any normal section of the shell will remain straight and 
normal to the mid-surface after bending deformation, as illustrated in Figure 3.5. This means that 
points T, M, and B will remain on a straight line orthogonal to the mid surface. The Kirchhoff-Love 
theory is a very simple bending theory, and it does not always perform well in areas of highly 
concentrated moments.

Some of the most common assumptions throughout the shell bending theories used in FEA are:

3.3 BUCKLING  
Buckling is an instability phenomenon, which occurs when slender structures are subjected to 
large axial or in-plane compression. Instability of a structure means that a small change in loading 
will cause a disproportionately large change in the shape of the structure.  
A well-known buckling case is the lateral buckling of an axially compressed column. At a certain 
load magnitude, the column will reach a state, where any small, transverse load or deflection will 
cause the column to deflect dramatically. The normal force at this state is called the critical or the 
buckling load. The concept of hypothetical transverse displacement is very much relevant, since 
real life structures will always have some geometrical deviations from the perfect, theoretical 
structure. The buckling load of a column, also called the Euler load, is found as: 
This expression illustrates that the buckling is very much dependent on the relationship between 
depth and length of the column, as e.g. in a rectangular column the moment of inertia is a cubic 
function of the depth. 
 
2
2
4 eff
cr
l
EI
P


 
( 3-5 )  
==PAGE:35==
 
3  THEORY OF SHELL STRUCTURES 
25 
 
Since shell structures carry transverse load through normal forces, buckling can occur whenever 
the loads cause the shell to have compressive membrane forces. The buckling load of shell 
buckling has a similar dependency of stiffness and span as for column buckling. 
Instability is a crucial part of shell design, since membrane behaviour of shells, necessitates very 
little thickness of the structure, which unfortunately makes them very sensitive to buckling. The 
dilemma of membrane behaviour vs. buckling behaviour is well summarised by Chris Williams: 
Shell buckling is particularly nasty because shell structures are so efficient. Almost 
no deflection occurs and then suddenly there is total collapse. Paradoxically, the 
less efficient the shell, in terms of shape, triangulations of the surface and boundary 
support, the better it behaves in buckling. (Adriaenssens, et al., 2014) 
What should be taken away from this is not that inefficient membrane shells are geometrically 
more resistant towards buckling, but that inefficient membrane shells will need larger thickness 
to resist membrane and bending forces, and will therefore have a higher buckling resistance. 
An important aspect of shell buckling is that it is typically a global phenomenon, which will cause 
the shell to collapse. This means that there is little point in considering post-buckling modes, i.e. 
what happens after the shell has buckled.  
On top of the buckling risk that immediately comes with shallow shell structures, the buckling of 
shell structures also carry a great sensitivity towards imperfections. This effect is worsened by the 
fact that the fabrication of shell structures is hard to control, whether that be pre-fab or in-situ. 
This means that the theoretical buckling loads will often be much greater than the actual buckling 
load in testing. 
Buckling analysis can be separated into two categories: linear and non-linear analysis. Linear vs. 
non-linear refers to geometrical linearity, i.e. whether only the original shape of the structure is 
analysed, or the deformation of the structure is tracked by performing an incremental analysis 
where the shape is updated after each load increment. Two examples of load-displacement curves 
are illustrated with both the linear and the non-linear buckling-behaviour in Figure 2.3.  
A characteristic of linear buckling analysis is the determination of a bifurcation point, the point 
where the structure can no longer sustain the current stability mode. The bifurcation point 
obviously marks the buckling load, and is a very tangible way to evaluate the resistance of the 
structure. Most linear analysis will not cover what happens after the structure has buckled, i.e. the 
post buckling behaviour.  
 
==PAGE:36==
 
3  THEORY OF SHELL STRUCTURES 
26 
 
 
Figure 3.6 - Examples of buckling behaviour 
Linear buckling is an eigenvalue problem, where the conventional stiffness matrix K is expanded 
with a geometrical stiffness matrix Kg. The geometrical stiffness matrix introduces second order 
effects, which means that the stiffness of the structure is dependent on the normal forces. The 
eigenvalue λ is also called the load factor and when multiplied with the applied load it gives the 
buckling load. V is the displacement field corresponding to the buckling load, also called the 
buckling mode. The system needed to be solved to find the buckling load is:  
Second order theory considers the fact that structures have an increase in bending stiffness 
when in tension and a decrease when in compression, which is at times referred to as stress 
stiffening and weakening. The concept of stress stiffening/weakening also helps the 
understanding of the eigenvalue buckling: considering that the bending stiffness of the structure 
reduces with increasing compression, at some compression level no bending resistance is left, 
which is what the buckling load represents. 
Typically, when performing linear buckling analysis a unit load will be applied, such that the load 
factor will be equal to the buckling load. Theoretically, an array of eigenvalues will yield buckling 
in the structure, but in practice, only the first, the lowest, will be interesting, since this leads to 
collapse of the shell.  
 
0
)
(


V
K
K
g

 
( 3-6 ) 
==PAGE:37==
 
3  THEORY OF SHELL STRUCTURES 
27 
 
Non-linear buckling analysis is the more realistic, but also immensely complex model of the 
deformations of a structure, and it does not always have a clear buckling load. The non-linear 
buckling analysis is typically performed as an incremental analysis, where instead of incrementing 
the load, the displacement is incremented, and the corresponding load is determined. The process 
of incrementing the deformation instead of the load, is important in cases like the arch/dome case 
of Figure 3.6, where more than one displacement exist at a certain load magnitudes. In some cases, 
such as columns and disks, the structure gradually loses stiffness, as illustrated in the top figure 
of Figure 3.6. In other cases, such as shallow arches and domes, the structure reaches a limit point 
and suddenly goes from one shape to a drastically different shape; this is called snap-through 
buckling. Cases also exist where the linear buckling analysis underestimates the actual buckling 
load. The types of non-linear buckling response are as many as there are structural variations, 
endless.  
The incremental analysis does not always lead to instability, and then the buckling has to be 
initiated with a very small imperfection load or displacement, to cause an eccentricity. This is the 
case for an in-plane loaded plate or an axially loaded column. In these cases, it is helpful to study 
the linear buckling mode, in order to imitate this deformation with an imperfection load or 
displacement. Another reason for studying the linear buckling deformation, before performing the 
non-linear analysis, is that in the non-linear analysis, the displacement must be tracked in a specific 
point, and if the wrong point is chosen, the buckling might not be detected. Even after performing 
non-linear buckling analysis, physical models of the structure will often be tested, in order to get 
a more realistic setup of geometrical, material and load imperfections.  
 
Linear buckling is a reasonable approximation for axially and in-plane loaded structures, but for 
shells, which buckle under transverse load, transverse displacements before buckling, can have a 
great impact on the buckling behaviour, reducing the buckling load significantly. The complexity 
of performing non-linear buckling analysis means that it is expedient using the linear buckling 
analysis in the initial stages of a design process, and postpone the non-linear analysis for the 
more detailed phases.  
The very concrete feedback of the linear buckling analysis makes it ideal for comparison of 
different structures, however, this should be done with caution. The relations between the linear 
and non-linear buckling loads of different structures are not equal, which is very distinct in the 
examples of Figure 3.6. For this reason, two general rules should be remembered, when drawing 
conclusions based on linear buckling analysis: The structures have to be of a similar nature and 
the differences in buckling load have to be significant. 
==PAGE:38==
 
4  METHODICAL TESTS 
28 
 
In this chapter, simple structures with analytical solutions will be used to test the analysis types, 
which will later be used for analysing the ø50 shell with openings. By testing Karamba on cases 
of e.g. pure membrane behaviour or pure bending, it is possible to evaluate the individual 
computational methods of Karamba. This creates a solid basis for assessing the origin of 
inaccuracies in the complex shell structure. 
The works of S.P. Timoshenko have been the primary source for the analytical cases. His work is 
seen by many as one of the cornerstones in structural engineering, and in this case, his 
publications on shell structures and instability have been of particular relevance. 
4.1 FRAMEWORK FOR ANALYSIS OF A SHELL WITH OPENINGS 
When working with shell structures the two main aspects to consider are section forces and 
buckling. More specifically the section forces concern the principal membrane forces and the 
principal moments as introduced previously. Bending is to be expected in a shell structure with 
openings, as it violates the membrane condition of a continuous shell. 
In the case of membrane forces, it is both relevant to test the accuracy of the membrane forces, 
but also the ability to approximate pure membrane conditions i.e. a bending free force field. Larger 
membrane forces should be expected in a shell with openings than in a continuous shell, since the 
forces have to be re-directed around the holes, especially in this case, where the openings are 
placed close to the edge.   
In terms of bending forces, it has been mentioned that bending theories used in FEA are subjects 
of more of less harsh approximation. In particular, the Kirchhoff-Love theory, which is the basis 
of the bending algorithm in Karamba, has been known to cause complications in the proximity of 
small holes and other moment concentrations (Nielsen, 2008).  
The determination of section forces through linear analysis will be denoted as stress analysis in 
the following. This will also cover the displacements, though these are rarely governing for shell 
structures. Since shell structures carry most of their load through membrane forces, they are much 
more prone to loosing stability than large displacements. Since buckling analysis is much more 
comprehensive than linear stress analysis, the displacements can sometimes be used as a measure 
of the stiffness of the structure.  
4 METHODICAL TESTS
==PAGE:39==
 
4  METHODICAL TESTS 
29 
 
The importance of buckling analysis for shell structures has already been covered, and with a shell 
of a rise to width ratio of approximately 1/10 and large openings as it is the case here, the stiffness 
is compromised. Performing a full non-linear analysis is the safer option, but with the analysis 
being difficult to set up, time consuming to run and the results needing experienced evaluation, 
there is a general tendency to avoid non-linear analysis, when it is possible. In many cases the 
linear buckling analysis will be used to determine whether buckling is critical for the structure or 
not, and if a non-linear analysis is needed. However, because of the diversity of shell structures 
and the complexity of buckling behaviour, no standards and very little guidance exist for making 
such assessment.  
An example of the precautions taken with respect to buckling is found in the Teshima Art Museum 
in Japan, which is a shell with openings with similar dimensions to the ø50 shell. The engineer 
Mutsuro Sasaki describes that safety factors of 3,14-5,23 were used on the non-linear buckling 
load, depending on the duration of the load (Adriaenssens, et al., 2014). 
With the lack of information on the subject, it is assumed that non-linear buckling analysis is 
necessary and that the linear buckling load should be expected to be at least 2-3 times the 
magnitude of the maximum load, for size reference. Instead, the focus of the linear buckling 
analysis will be the application as a design tool. By having a trustworthy linear buckling analysis, 
the stiffness of different design variations can be tested, and in most cases the linear buckling 
analysis will give a good approximation of the buckling mode. 
 
Besides the stress and buckling analysis, it is desirable to explore the options for analysing the 
deformations around the openings. It is hard to fully predict what happens when a flow of 
membrane forces is interrupted with an opening. High force and therefore strain concentrations 
will occur, the support conditions for the edge segments will be challenged etc. One theory of the 
edge effects when cutting holes in a membrane shell is presented by Chris Williams (Adriaenssens, 
et al., 2014), namely inextensional deformation.  
Inextensional deformation is a form of deformation, which causes no strain. This is very much a 
juxtaposition to the deformation of a continuous dome, which is fully supported at the edges, since 
no deformation can occur without straining of the mid-surface. The inextensional deformation 
occurs in structures, which are not supported properly, which is for example the case in rigid body 
movement. Williams suggest that such a behaviour occurs at the edges of openings in dome shells, 
when the holes are large compared to the thickness, when the bending stiffness is small. This 
would be caused by insufficient support through membrane forces in the edge segments of the 
shell, as illustrated in Figure 4.1. 
==PAGE:40==
 
4  METHODICAL TESTS 
30 
 
            
 
          
 
Figure 4.1 ‒ Supporting membrane forces of shell segment 
Another angle on how the edges might deform can be achieved by imagining the edge of the 
opening as a ring beam, which carries a much higher concentration of normal forces than the rest 
of the shell. When the ring beam is compressed, the circumference of the hole is reduced, forcing 
the ring downwards, as illustrated in Figure 4.2, where d>d’. Such a behaviour is unlikely to be 
noticeable in a linear analysis, but could possibly have an effect in a non-linear analysis, where the 
ring will be compressed, then transversally loaded, over and over. 
 
Figure 4.2 ‒ Lowering of edge ring from compression strains 
The exact type of the deformations will not be considered, but the two examples have been 
included to illustrate the complexity of deformations in a shell with openings. This means that 
geometrically non-linear analysis and other specialised tool would be helpful in exploring the 
deformations. 
Throughout the tests, compression forces will be counted as negative and tension forces as 
positive. The moments are generally considered positive when they cause compression in the 
lower side, i.e. upwards bending, however this will depend on the local coordinate system. The 
analysis results will generally be evaluated by looking at the maximum and minimum values of 
the relevant forces and displacements, disregarding the placement of the values.  
==PAGE:41==
 
4  METHODICAL TESTS 
31 
 
4.2 STRESS ANALYSIS 
The most common analysis component in Karamba is the AnalyzeThI component, which is shown 
in Figure 4.3, which performs first order (linear) analysis on the assembled model. From the 
analysed model, an array of different outputs can be extracted either automatically or manually. 
Most relevant are the shell section forces and the displacement. The shell forces component 
returns the principal membrane forces N1 and N2 and the principal moment M1 and M2 per 
element. All section forces will be given as force per unit length along the middle surface parallel 
to the section, i.e. normal forces as kN/m and moments as kNm/m=kN. 
The analysis does not return the transversal shear forces, but for good reason, as these will be of 
very little importance for the strength of the shell. The nodal displacement component returns the 
translation and rotation of each node. 
 
Figure 4.3 - First order analysis in Karamba 
4.2.1 MEMBRANE FORCES 
In order to test the methods used for calculating membrane forces in a shell structure, the case of 
a full spherical cap under evenly distributed load is used. An analytical solution exists with pure 
membrane behaviour, when the edge of the shell is supported tangentially to the mid-surface, as 
seen in Figure 4.4. The overall dimensions of the ø50 shell are chosen for consistency.  
 
Figure 4.4 - Spherical cap under distributed load 
When speaking of spherical shells two important geometrical terms must be introduced: Parallel 
circles and meridian, these are illustrated in Figure 4.5a. The meridian is the arc, which is the 
intersection between the spherical surface and a plane parallel to the axis of rotation. The parallel 
circles are the circles, which are produced as intersections between the spherical surface and 
planes orthogonal to the axis of rotation. These two terms are important, because the principal 
==PAGE:42==
 
4  METHODICAL TESTS 
32 
 
membrane forces of the spherical shell under distributed load, follows the direction of respectively 
the meridian and the parallel circle. The force in the meridian direction is commonly called the 
meridional force, and the one in the direction of the parallel circle is called the hoop force. 
Since both the geometry, the load and the supports are rotational symmetric, the forces will also 
have rotational symmetry, and will only vary along the meridian, not along the parallel circles. The 
principal forces in a point can therefore be found as a function of the angle α between the radius 
of curvature, R, and the axis of rotation, as illustrated in Figure 4.5b. The angle about the axis of 
rotation, i.e. along the parallel circles, is denoted θ, though this will only be used for notation of 
the hoop force in this case. 
 
(a) Meridian and parallel circle 
(b) Meridional angle parameter and radius 
of curvature 
Figure 4.5 ‒ Geometrical terms of spherical shell 
The principal forces of the spherical shell are described by Csonka (1987) as:

The meridional force is of increasing compression from the top towards the bottom. The hoop 
force is a compressional force for angles α<π/4, which means that in this case where the angle 
at the bottom edge is 0,42 radians, the hoop force will also be compressional throughout the shell. 
For comparison with the analytical solution a Karamba model is set up with the properties of Table 
4.1, loaded with pure self-weight. The Karamba model is shown with mesh and support directions 
==PAGE:43==

in Figure 4.6. The default mesh created in Grasshopper when meshing a spherical surface is 
concentric, such that meridians and parallel circles are used to subdivide the surface.

Since the analytical solution to the spherical shell is based on the shell being able to carry all 
transversal loads through membrane forces, no bending should occur. However a finite element 
model with plane elements, will naturally introduce some bending. In order to check the quality 
of the membrane behaviour of the shell, the principal moments are determined similarly to the 
principal membrane forces. As the moments are mostly a result of bending within the elements, 
the principal moment directions do not follow a symmetric pattern as the membrane forces do, 
they will therefore be referred to as M1 and M2. The principal moments are listed in Table 4.4.

Table 4.4 - Moments in membrane analysis of spherical shell 
As expected, some moments occur in the membrane shell, and they are greatly reduced by 
increasing the number of elements. The reduction of the moments is also illustrated in Graph 4.2. 
In the context of plate bending within the planar elements, it is worth noticing that the majority 
of the moments occur at the bottom of the shell, where the elements are largest.  
The approximated geometry will also induce moments, which should be most outspoken in the 
top where the triangular elements end in a point, and at the bottom where the tangential supports 
are not perfectly tangential. This would also explain why the extreme moments primarily occur 
around the top and the bottom of the shell.  
==PAGE:46==
 
4  METHODICAL TESTS 
36 
 
 
Graph 4.2 ‒ Moments in membrane analysis of spherical shell 
Since the theoretical value for the moments is zero, it is not possible to rate the moments purely 
from the analytical solution. The size of the moments is assessed by comparing the principal 
stresses at the top, middle and bottom of the shell at the points of numerically largest moments, 
i.e. max M1 and min M2. The principal stresses are determined similarly to the principal section 
forces, only in this case the layer of stress much be specified as a factor between -1 and 1, where 
-1 indicates the bottom layer, 0 the mid-surface, and 1 the top layer. The principal stresses are 
denoted σ1 and σ2. If the pure membrane behaviour was fulfilled the stress would be the same 
throughout the thickness of the shell.  
The principal stresses do not necessarily follow the direction of the principal moments, as the 
principal stresses of respectively the top and bottom layer of the shell are both affected by 
membrane forces and moments. However, it gives an indication of the magnitude of the moments. 
The principal stresses of the three layers are given for maximum M1 in Table 4.5 and for minimum 
M2 in Table 4.6. The maximum deviation shows the larger of the two differences in stress, top-
middle and bottom-middle.

Table 4.6 - Stress variation over the thickness in points of min M2 
The comparison of the top and centre stresses shows that although the moments of Table 4.4 
seem quite small, they are not completely insignificant. The stress variation over the thickness 
naturally reduces with the number of elements, due to the reduction of the moment.   
Moments cannot be avoided altogether when approximating a shell structure with planar 
elements. However, they can be reduced significantly by using a fine mesh, and stress variations 
of 5%, will not be decisive for the geometry of the structure.  
Both the membrane forces and the moments show drastic improvements after the two first 
iterations, hence it seems that the method Karamba uses for calculating membrane forces is good, 
but the quality of the results is very sensitive to the number of elements. 
4.2.1.1 DEFORMATION 
The deformation of the membrane shell is much less often addressed than the membrane forces. 
This suggests that there is not one commonly accepted solution to the problem. The solution 
==PAGE:48==
 
4  METHODICAL TESTS

chosen in this case is described by Farshad (1992), who presents a solution to the displacements 
in respectively the meridional direction and the normal direction of the shell as a function of the 
angle α. In (4-3) the solution has been rewritten to give the maximum displacement, i.e. the 
vertical displacement δ at the top, where αsup is the angle at the supports. As the analytical model 
is bending free, all normal displacements occur as results of membrane strains.

As the deviations show, the displacements are not extremely accurate, which means that it is not 
advisable to use them for detailed dimensioning of the shell. This does not necessarily mean that 
the displacements are wrong, only that the calculation behind Karamba and the analytical solution 
are different. As previously mentioned, deflections will rarely be governing for shell structures, 
and mostly be used to indicate the stiffness of the structure. For this purpose, it is mainly needed 
for the results to be consistent, i.e. for the deviations to be of similar size with different geometries, 
loads etc. This can only be determined by running an array of analyses, but an indication of 
consistent results, is given through convergence of the results with mesh refinement, which is 
seen in Graph 4.3.

4.2.2 BENDING 
A simple bending test of a cantilever beam is carried out, in order to test the functionality of 
bending of shell elements. A cantilever beam as seen in Figure 4.7 is modelled twice in Karamba 
with respectively beam and shell elements, in order to detect whether possible deviations are 
caused by the solver or the element type. The two models have all the same dimensions, and the 
properties of Table 4.8. 
 
Figure 4.7 - Cantilever beam 
The maximum values of the moment, the resulting stress and the displacement are found at 
respectively the support and the free end as: 
The cantilever beam is analysed with a varying number of divisions over the length for both the 
beam and shell model. The shell model is not divided in the width of the beam, but it should be 
noted that since the shell elements are triangular, there are actually twice as many shell elements 
as beam elements. In table Table 4.9 the moments, stresses and displacements of the beam and 
shell models are compared to the analytical results.

Graph 4.4 ‒ Deviations of shell model results for bending of a cantilever beam 
The results show that the beam model follow the analytical solution completely, meaning that the 
deviations of the shell model can be tied to the shell elements. As expected, the moments and 
stresses are more or less proportional for all the results.  
In Graph 4.4 the deviations of moments and displacements of the shell model are displayed, the 
beam results are left out, since they have approximately zero deviation. In spite of the simple 
nature of the structure, the shell elements do need a fairly fine mesh for the forces to converge, 
however when they do, they are very accurate. This verifies the method used for calculation of 
bending in shell elements, but it is advisable to perform some sort of convergence analysis, to 
ensure that a sufficiently fine meshing is used, especially because here the forces are increasing 
with the number of elements. It should be noted that the deflections converge towards a value 
slightly lower than the analytical value, but compared to the deviations of the membrane 
deformation, 0,8% is an insignificant error.

4.3 BUCKLING 
Karamba has a Buckling Modes component, which performs Eigenvalue buckling analysis. The 
analysis is performed in two steps. First, a second order analysis has to be carried out, and then 
he buckling analysis can be performed on the model, based on the second order analysis, as shown 
in Figure 4.8. As a default, the buckling analysis outputs the first buckling mode, with the 
correspond Buckling load factor, but it is possible to specify the number of buckling modes for 
the output.   
The buckling component uses a fairly simple iteration method to solve the Eigenvalue problem, 
which yields that if two buckling modes have very similar buckling loads, the solver can have a 
hard time distinguishing the separate buckling modes. When this happens, the number of 
maximum iterations can be increased, but this will also increase the computational time. The 
buckling analyses of this study will generally be performed with fewer elements than the stress 
analysis, as they are much more computational heavy. 
 
Figure 4.8 ‒ Buckling analysis in Karamba 
Karamba is unfortunately not able to perform non-linear buckling analysis, which is generally the 
tendency for more simplistic FE software, because of the computational gravity of the analyses. 
This gives great limitations for the detail level to which Karamba can be used.  
4.3.1 SECOND ORDER ANALYSIS 
Based on the load applied to the model, the second order analysis builds up a geometrical stiffness 
matrix for the corresponding membrane force distribution. When this is done, the job of the 
buckling analysis is to solve the eigenvalue problem. 
A structure, which illustrates a second order effect, is the beam-column, as seen in Figure 4.9. In 
a first order analysis, the axial force P will have no effect on the bending of the beam-column, but 
with a second order analysis, the bending stiffness is reduced by the compression and the 
deflection will be increased. This might be counterintuitive since we normally consider the 
transverse load to be weakening for the normal force in such a case, but shifting the point of view, 
the normal force is equally weakening for the bending stiffness. This example can also help the 
==PAGE:52==
 
4  METHODICAL TESTS 
42 
 
understanding of column buckling, as the buckling load can be considered as the value for P, 
where an infinitesimal value of Q will make the beam deflect drastically.  
 
Figure 4.9 - Beam-column for second order analysis 
Timoshenko (1961) describes an analytical solution, to the problem of a beam-column with axial 
load P and a transversal point load Q at the middle of the beam. The maximum displacement, δmax, 
and moment, Mmax, are both found at the middle of the beam as:

4.3.2 BUCKLING OF AN ARCH 
A simple case of buckling of a transversally loaded structure is tested through the case of an arch 
with a uniform pressure load, as seen in Figure 4.10.  
 
Figure 4.10 ‒ Uniformly compressed arch 
The arch can be viewed both as a curved beam and as a section of a cylinder, either way it is a 
singly curved structure, with a more or less two-dimensional stress field. 
Timoshenko [1961] has given an analytical solution to the buckling load, for the case of a beam 
and a shell respectively, with the main difference being that expansion in the width of the arch is 
included with poisson’s ratio, ν, in the shell solution: 
Timoshenko mentions that in reality, flatter arches are more affected by the compression of the 
arch up until buckling occurs, which leads to snap-through buckling, but since the pre-buckling 
response is not covered in the linear buckling analysis, this effect is disregarded. The chosen 
analytical solution has an asymmetric buckling mode as illustrated in Figure 4.11. 
 
Figure 4.11 ‒ Analytical buckling mode of arch

4.3.3 BUCKLING OF SPHERICAL CAP 
In order to test the performance of the buckling analysis in a three-dimensional stress field, a 
spherical cap with uniform pressure and clamped edges, as seen in Figure 4.13, is introduced. The 
spherical cap is one of the more complex structures, for which an analytical buckling load has 
been identified. 
 
Figure 4.13 ‒ Spherical cap with uniform pressure 
Timoshenko also described the analytical buckling load for the sphere in Theory of Elastic Stability 
(1961): 
The solution is based on an assumption of axis symmetric buckling deformation. 
It is chosen to test the shell with different thicknesses, to see if the program has a harder time 
calculating the buckling loads for shells, which are more or less sensitive to buckling. The test are 
still carried out with a varying number of elements, the results of which can be seen in Table 4.15.

In addition to the buckling load, it is also noted, whether the buckling load is of a local or a global 
type. Two examples of buckling modes are included in Figure 4.14.  
The type of buckling mode is included in the table in order to see if any of the inaccurate buckling 
loads are results of incorrect buckling modes. The results show that with thinner shells of coarse 
meshes, there is a risk of artificial buckling modes occurring. However, these will most likely be 
on the safe side, since the large, plane elements of the FE model are the cause of the stiffness 
reduction. 
 
(a) 50mm shell with 600 elements (deformations x 100) 
==PAGE:58==
 
4  METHODICAL TESTS 
48 
 
 
(b) 50mm shell with 5400 elements (deformations x 1) 
Figure 4.14 ‒ Examples of buckling modes from Karamba 
The results are slightly more accurate the thinner the shell is. Additionally, the calculations of the 
thicker shells necessitates more iterations than the thinner shells, i.e. the computational time is 
longer, which indicates that the buckling modes of the stiffer shells are harder to distinguish from 
each other. In practice, shells of great buckling stiffness, will be likely to suffer from another type 
of failure mode before buckling. 
The major deviations in buckling load are tied to the local buckling modes, which are quite 
distinctive from the graphical representation of the deviations in Graph 4.7. This means that it is 
helpful to have some idea of the buckling mode going in to the analysis.. As mentioned previously, 
deviations 2-4% are perfectly acceptable, since the subject of linear buckling of shells is highly 
inaccurate.
