4.4 LARGE DEFORMATIONS 
Karamba has an algorithm for analysing structures with large deformations, i.e. geometric non-
linearity. The component separates the load into a set number of increments and updates the 
geometry after each load step. It is a fairly simple non-linear analysis, as there is no iteration 
towards equilibrium in each step. The maker of Karamba describes this as a purely incremental 
analysis (Preisinger, 2015), rather than a non-linear analysis. The number of increments can be 
increased, in order to obtain a smoother set of results; however, it does not ensure that the results 
converges towards the results of the non-linear solution.  
 
Figure 4.15 ‒ Large deformations component 
From the picture of the Large Deformations component in Figure 4.15, it can be seen that the 
component outputs a model and a displacement. The output model is the deformed model, which 
currently does not return any information of the forces, stresses or strains obtained from the 
deformation. This is caused by the logic of the incremental analysis used, which discards 
information of stresses, when incrementing the load, only keeping the information of the 
displacements. The displacement output is the maximum nodal displacement. 
In the following tests, it will be attempted to determine section forces and stresses by performing 
a linear analysis on the deformed model. 
4.4.1 BEAM‐COLUMN DEFLECTION 
The beam column case used for testing the second order analysis in section 4.3.1, is also used for 
testing the large deformations analysis. 
Since the section forces are not calculated with the Large deformations component in 
Karamba, the moments are found by performing a simple, linear analysis on the deformed 
model. This is not far from how the moment is determined in the analytical solution, hence 
why this procedure is likely to give reasonable section forces. 
The Karamba analysis has been carried out with one mesh refinement and a varying number of 
increments, the results of which can be seen in Table 4.16. The results of the first and second 
order analyses are also included for comparison.

4.4.2 PLATE WITH LARGE DEFLECTION 
In the basic analysis of plate bending, plates are assumed to carry transversal loads through pure 
bending. This is a result of the forces being calculated from the undeformed geometry, which is 
an acceptable simplification in the case of small deformations. In the case of large deformations 
however, the plate will no longer be approximately plane, and membrane forces will occur.  
Timoshenko (1976) has found an approximation to the non-linear case of an evenly loaded, 
rectangular plate with clamped edges, as seen in Figure 4.16. 
 
Figure 4.16 - Evenly loaded plate with clamped edges 
Timoshenko has found approximations to the maximum displacement, found in the middle of the 
plate, in the case of plates with Poisson’s ratio of ν=0,3. The non-linear displacement of the plate 
with a variety of width to length ratios, b/a, are displayed in  Figure 4.17. The relation b/a=0 
represents an infinitely long plate. Along with the approximations to the non-linear case of large 
displacements, the linear solutions for small displacements are also displayed in the graph. The 
two sets of solutions display the obvious relation that the smaller the displacements are, the closer 
the non-linear solution is to the linear. 
==PAGE:62==
 
4  METHODICAL TESTS 
52 
 
 
Figure 4.17 ‒ Displacements in plate with large displacements by Timoshenko [1976] 
From the displacements, Timoshenko has determined the maximum stress as illustrated in Figure 
4.18, which occurs at the middle of the long edges of the plate, marked with red in Figure 4.16. 
Timoshenko himself states that the approximated stresses are slightly higher than the actual 
stresses. In both of the graphs, the values used for the analyses are marked with red. 
 
Figure 4.18  - Stresses in plate with large displacements by Timoshenko [1976] 
The factor Sy must be found from the graph of Figure 4.18, and then the maximum stress can be 
found as:

most determining factor for the non-linear deformations. 
In order to see some difference between the linear and large deformation analysis, a 40mm thick 
concrete plate with a minimum span of 2m is loaded with 500kN/m2. This is obviously not a 
realistic situation, especially without considering reinforcement, but since the strength of the 
material is not included in this analysis and the material is considered linear elastic, it is perfectly 
acceptable as a theoretical example. The test is carried out with three different ratios of b/a, b/a=1, 
b/a=2/3 and b/a=1/8. For both the displacements and the stress, the results of plates longer than 
b/a=2/3 comes fairly close to the ones of the infinitely long plate, b/a=0, hence why in the 
following it is chosen to consider a plate of b/a=1/8 infinitely long, i.e. b/a=1/8=0.

with one mesh refinement a varying number of load increments.

Graph 4.9 ‒ Deviations of displacements of plate with large deflections 
The displacements are the main focus of this analysis, since these are a part of the output of the 
large deformations component. In Graph 4.9 the deviations of the displacements from the 
analytical results are shown. The results show that clear reductions in the displacements are 
achieved by introducing increments to the analysis, but the deviations are still large. This means 
that the component can be used for investigating large deformation effects, but a great emphasise 
should not be put on the values of the displacement. The deviations of the plate of a=b are slightly 
lower than the other two plates, which could indicate that symmetrical deformations are more 
easily approximated. 
The stresses found from linear analysis of the deformed plate are drastically lower than the 
analytical stresses. The stress analysis is performed by applying the full load to the deformed 
model, which leads to an additional deformation of the plate, and does not include the stresses 
developed throughout the large deformation analysis. It is therefore not advisable to use the large 
displacement analysis for stress analysis. 
The tests of the Large Deformations analysis show that different kinds of geometrically non-linear 
behaviour can be imitated, though not with a great accuracy level. The application of the analysis 
is however very limited, as there is no proper way of analysing the forces, stresses or strains. This

means that non-linear deformations can be discovered, but the effects of the deformations cannot

4.5 DEFORMATION ENERGY 
Karamba has a Deformation Energy component, which determines the strain energy of the 
structure after deformation. The component takes in the analysed model and outputs respectively 
the axial and the bending deformation energy of each element, which can be seen from Figure 
4.19. The total deformation energy of the system is also provided with the linear analysis 
component, which is the sum of both axial and bending energy of all the elements. 
 
Figure 4.19 ‒ Deformation Energy and Linear Analysis components. 
The total axial and bending deformation energy of the structure, can be used as a measurement 
for the stiffness of the structure. This is useful, since the maximum displacement does not paint a 
very clear picture of the overall deformations of a structure. The most general application of 
deformation energy is that the smaller the total deformation energy is, the more efficient the 
structure is, i.e. the less the structure deforms. For the application of shell structures, it is also 
relevant to differentiate axial and bending energy, since pure axial deformation and no bending 
deformation is the ideal for a shell structure. This way, the quality of different shell designs could 
be compared, with the help of the deformation energy analysis. 
The distribution of the axial and the bending energy gives the distribution of the combined 
principal forces, such that the most critical areas of the structure can be found with respect to 
membrane forces and moments.  
It should be noted that a bug was found in the program, which means that the deformation energy 
should not be calculated from the automatically generated Gravity load. This is easily worked 
around when working with a constant shell thickness, as it can be substituted for one surface load, 
but is slightly more inconvenient, when working with varying thicknesses. 
==PAGE:66==
 
4  METHODICAL TESTS 
56

possible case, a centrally loaded column, as seen in Figure 4.20. For the case of pure axial strain, 
buckling must be disregarded.

5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
61 
 
In this chapter, the accuracy level of Karamba is studied in the case of a shell with openings, where 
there is no analytical solution. For this purpose, Robot Structural Analysis has been chosen for the 
comparison of the results. Even though the performance of Robot is not the focus of this study, 
the Karamba-Robot comparison will be initiated with the case of the continuous spherical cap also 
used in Chapter 4. By comparing the Karamba and Robot results to the analytical solutions, it is 
possible to catch any major differences in the setup of the models and get a slight idea of the 
accuracy level of Robot. 
The analyses pivots around the ø50 shell introduced in section 1.3, and the main variable 
throughout the analyses is the number of elements. It is assumed that the thickness and material 
stiffness will not have an effect on the accuracy of the results, as they should not have an effect 
on the computational methods of the program. Since the quality and the strength of the structure 
is not in focus at this point, the generic material and cross section properties of Table 5.1 is used 
throughout the analyses of this chapter.  
Properties 
  Material 
Concrete C12/15
  E 
27000
MPa 
  ν 
0,2
  
  Thickness 
100
mm 
  Self-weight 
2,5
kN/m2 
  R 
61,08
m 
Table 5.1- Properties of shell analysis 
Only evenly distributed loads have been used for the analyses, since these are assumed governing 
for the shell. Snow loads on shells can be complicated to model, since the inclination is not 
constant, and in this case, where the shell is fairly flat, it would typically be assumed to be evenly 
distributed as well. Wind load has very little effect on a shallow shell, and with the complexity of 
modelling wind on a shell with openings, it is chosen not to include it. 
 
For all the analyses a convergence analyses will be carried out, to ensure that consistent results 
across different levels of mesh refinement can be achieved. The specific number of elements at 
convergence will not be of much interest, more so the size of the deviations of the results when 
they converge. The mesh refinement is generally stopped at a point, where Robot is no longer able 
5 COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT 
SOFTWARE 
==PAGE:72==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
62 
 
to refine the mesh, and at that point, the promptitude of the Karamba analysis is also being 
compromised.  
As FEA is a method of approximation, a 0% deviation of the Karamba results from the Robot results, 
does not mean perfectly accurate results, it means perfect resemblance of computational methods, 
which is the best possible measurement, when no analytical solution exists.  
5.1 MESHING 
The meshing of the shell has shown to be a major consideration when performing FEA on a shell 
with openings. This topic is important to study, since meshing is already a major field within 
Grasshopper. As the meshes are being put to new use, new demands occur, and it is crucial to 
consider, how the mesh will work for the specific structure in terms of geometry, support, loads, 
force distribution, deformations etc., since the structure will only be evaluated at the nodes. 
Normally when meshing in Grasshopper, only the geometry has to be considered. 
In Robot, an automatically constructed mesh with triangular elements has been used throughout 
the analyses. For the sake of comparison, it has been aimed to achieve a similar mesh in Karamba 
to those obtained with Robot. Two main issues related to meshing in Karamba were encountered: 
Mesh lines almost colliding with openings and mesh approximations that manipulate the shape of 
the shell. 
The first of the problems relates to meshes of a linear nature, i.e. meshes where the divisions can 
be made with planes, such that the element edges form plane, continuous curves. The problem 
occurs, when the mesh lines almost tangent the openings, which leads to a stress concentration, 
not occuring at other levels of mesh refinement. The stress concentrations are assumably a result 
of the very oddly shaped elements in the area where the mesh line and the edge curve almost 
adjoin, marked with a red arrow in Figure 5.1. With meshes where this phenomenon occurs, a 
convergence analysis is always necessary, in order to identify the diverging results.  
The second problem occurs, when an attempt is made to obtain a more random element 
distribution. Some functions available for creating random or scrambled meshes in Grasshopper, 
treats the surface definition softly, meaning that they do not only modify the topology of the mesh, 
but also the geometry. The consequence of this modification is particularly pronounced, when 
tested on a shell with tangential supports, since a change in inclination of the shell at the edge, 
will cause the supports to induce bending. Generally, the shape manipulation is not ideal, because 
shells are known to be very shape sensitive.  
==PAGE:73==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
63 
 
         
 
Figure 5.1 ‒ Stress concentration due to mesh lines tangent to the openings 
The mesh types tested in Karamba can be separated into three main categories: 
Top centralised: The top centralised mesh is the mesh type used in the methodical 
tests of a spherical cap in section 4. This was quickly eliminated from the following 
tests, partially because it leads to a large amount of elements at the top, which is 
pointless once the geometry is no longer rotational symmetric, additionally it was 
aimed to achieve a mesh more similar to the more evenly sized mesh of Robot.  
Square: The square mesh covers meshes, which consist of seemingly equally sized 
square elements split into triangles at the diagonal. The square mesh has the 
regularity of the top centralised mesh, but a more uniform element size. The major 
downside to the square mesh is edge grazing mesh lines as in Figure 5.1. 
Remeshing: A large group of meshing tools offered for the Grasshopper interface 
work from the principal of redistributing the nodes within a regular mesh from a 
mathematical set of rules. This can lead to a more scrambled placement of the 
elements. A downside to some of these functions is that they will not only 
redistribute within the surface, they will also alter the shape of the mesh. It should 
also be noted that the remeshing-type functions add computational time. 
From these groups three different mesh types have been chosen to illustrate the effect, which the 
choice of mesh can have on the analysis results. The Square mesh is a generic mesh of 
quadrilaterals placed in rows, which have then been seperated into triangular elements. The 
Laplacian mesh is of the remeshing type, where the square mesh has been put through a 
remeshing tool, which is based on the Laplacian smoothing algorithm. With coarse meshes, the 
curvature of the Laplacian mesh can differ significantly from the original surface, but the shape 
approximation improves with mesh refinement. The Karamba mesh is build with a meshing tool, 
which comes with Karamba. The Karamba meshing tool both builds the generic mesh and 
performs remeshing operations, in such a way that the mesh differs minymally from the original 
surface. 
==PAGE:74==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
64 
 
The three mesh types are depicted on the ø50 shell alongside the Robot mesh in Figure 5.2.      
 
(a) Robot mesh 
(b) Square mesh 
 
(c) Laplacian mesh 
(d) Karamba mesh 
Figure 5.2 - Mesh types 
5.2 CONTINUOUS SPHERICAL CAP 
The continuous spherical cap tested in the Methodical tests of Section 4 is reintroduced, in order 
to get some understanding of the performance of Robot. In getting to know a FEM-program, it is 
helpful to start out with a structure, where the results are already familiar. By knowing the results, 
it is possible to catch any major errors in the setup of the model with respect to i.e. analysis types, 
loads, supports and meshing. 
5.2.1 STRESS ANALYSIS  
The stress analysis of the continuous shell is in principal identical to that in Section 4.2.1, only 
here, the different mesh types of Karamba are tested along with Robot. The analysis is carried out 
purely with self-weight. By using the same overall dimensions of the ø50 shell, the idea has been 
==PAGE:75==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
65 
 
to have the openings as the only change in the structure, which can affect the quality of the results, 
when the ø50 shell is analysed. However, an extra variation lies within the supports, as the 
analytical model has tangential supports, and simple supports are chosen to imitate more realistic 
conditions for the ø50 shell. 
The tangential supports necessary to obtain the pure membrane forces of the analytical model are 
rarely used in real life application. Luckily, the supports can be modelled in Robot, since the 
tangents at the edge nodes all point in the same direction. However, non-generic supports will 
generally be much easier to achieve in the parametric environment, which Karamba works in. 
It is chosen to model the tangential supports, such that they tangent the original surface and not 
the mesh. This especially has an influence on the Laplacian mesh, where the curvature of the shell 
is smoothened out.  
Contrary to the previous tests, only a selection of the results of the Robot-Karamba comparison 
will be included in this report, for further interest the full set of results can be found in Appendix 
A.1.1. 
In Graph 5.1 and Graph 5.2 the convergence of the Robot results and the results of the three 
Karamba mesh types are illustrated for the extremes of the principal membrane force N1, which 
corresponds to the hoop force and N2, which corresponds to the meridional force. The analytical 
values determined in 4.2.1 are marked with dashed lines.

Graph 5.2 - Principal membrane force N2 (meridional) in continuous shell 
The max hoop force, N1, which occurs at the bottom of the shell, clearly shows the Laplacian mesh 
as the worst fit. This indicates that there is a great risk involved in approximating the shape, in 
particular when the supports are tailored to the original shape. This effect is much less noticeable 
for the minimum value, which occurs at the top of the shell, where the Karamba mesh and Robot 
results lie perfectly behind the analytical line. Still, the Laplacian results display the slowest 
convergence, in all probability due to the change in shape with each mesh refinement.  
For the maximum meridional force, N2, occurring at the top, the values are slightly more uneven, 
but still, the convergence is quick. The minimum values, occurring at the bottom of the shell, 
display a very similar behaviour to that of maximum N1. 
For both membrane forces the results of the Square mesh and the Karamba mesh converge around 
7000 elements, at which point the calculations are very quick and unproblematic. The Robot 
results converge even faster, which is a great base for comparison in the more complex analysis. 
As mentioned previously, the analytical solution to the spherical cap entails zero moment, but the 
Finite Element models introduce moments, due to the planar elements. In Graph 5.3, the minimum 
and maximum values of the principal moment M2 are illustrated. For the minimum M2, typically 
occurring close to the edge, the Laplacian mesh again shows large deviations compared to the 
other models, which seems logical when the edge of the mesh is inclined differently than the 
supports. The two other mesh types and the Robot model follow very similar paths of convergence 
towards zero, which substantiate the point that the moments are tied to the facetted geometry,

Table 5.5 - Karamba mesh results of principle moments in continuous shell 
In Graph 5.3, the Robot and Karamba mesh results of M2 appeared to have a great resemblance, 
but the deviations between the Robot and the Karamba mesh moments are quite significant, which 
is a result of the moments being very small. As the Karamba moments are smaller than the Robot 
moments are, the Karamba results are in fact more accurate.  
In Graph 5.4 and Table 5.6 the displacements of the three mesh types and Robot are displayed 
along with the analytical solution of section 4.2.1.1. All the results converge towards a value 11% 
lower than the analytical solution. This again indicates that there is a computational difference 
between the analytical solution and the FE results. The uniformity of the results of all four models, 
suggests that there is a consistency in the Karamba calculations of displacements, meaning that 
the displacements can reasonably be used for comparing the stiffness of different designs. Also in 
this case, the Laplacian mesh shows the slowest convergence, which could be cause by reduced 
stiffness from the smoothened curvature of the mesh. It is worth noticing that the imperfect FE 
shells with bending moments deflect less than the analytical solution, i.e. the theoretically perfect 
membrane shell.

5.2.2 BUCKLING ANALYSIS 
The buckling analysis of the continuous spherical cap is also based on the same model as in the 
methodical tests, section 4.3.3. It should be noted that the buckling analysis of the spherical cap 
is performed with clamped edges, since this corresponds to the common analytical solution. This 
means that the supports do not have a direction, as it was the case for the stress analysis. 
The convergence of the buckling load of the Robot model and the three Karamba mesh types is 
illustrated in Graph 5.5. The full set of results can be found in Appendix A.1.2.  
 
Graph 5.5 - Buckling analysis of continuous shell

All of the four sets of buckling loads converge nicely towards the analytical solution, marked with 
a dashed line. Surprisingly, the Karamba results converge faster than those from Robot. 
Since none of the three Karamba models perform significantly better than the others do, the 
deviations in Table 5.7 are calculated for the Robot and the Karamba mesh models, for the sake 
of consistency. The Karamba mesh buckling loads are of extremely high accuracy, however they 
do converge towards a value slightly higher than the analytical solution. Though the deviations of 
the Robot results are higher, a deviation of 2,3% would still be considered very accurate, in relation 
to the approximate field of buckling analysis.

Table 5.7 ‒ Robot and Karamba mesh buckling loads for continuous shell 
In Figure 5.3 a selection of the buckling modes, which are found with the Karamba mesh can be 
seen. The first buckling mode, which is a definite fluctuation in Graph 5.5, is a case of very local 
buckling, as it was the case in the methodical tests. The buckling mode turns into a more global 
phenomenon with reduction of the element size. The analytical solution to this buckling problem 
stated that the buckling deformation would be symmetrical, which is not completely true for the 
finest mesh tested. This indicates that even perfectly regular shells have a number of possible 
buckling modes with very similar buckling loads. The asymmetric buckling could be sparked by a 
slight asymmetry in the mesh. 
 
 
==PAGE:83==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
73 
 
 
 
 
 
 
Figure 5.3 - Buckling modes of continuous shell with Karamba mesh 
5.3 SHELL WITH OPENINGS 
Once the quality of the analyses performed on the continuous shells have been investigated, it is 
time to introduce openings to the shell. The ø50 shell of Section 1.3 has been modelled as 
illustrated in Figure 5.4. In this study the foundation with a possible pre-tensioning has been 
excluded, instead the shell is provided with pinned supports around the entire edge, assuming that 
the foundation will be able to restrain all translations and no rotations. A thorough analysis of the 
shell would have to include the foundation, however this is outside the scope of this study.  
The holes are cut into the shell with cylinders, leading to perfectly circular holes, when looking at 
the shell from the top, but three-dimensional edge curves. In this case, it is chosen to prioritise 
easy modelling in both Karamba and Robot, over the construction issues that might come with the 
three-dimensional edge curves of the holes.  
526 elements 
1966 elements 
4321 elements 
7660 elements 
11506 elements 
==PAGE:84==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
74 
 
 
Figure 5.4 - ø50 shell for FE model 
5.3.1 STRESS ANALYSIS  
The stress analysis of the shell with openings is carried out with pure self-weight, with the 
properties listed in the beginning of the chapter. 
In Graph 5.6-Graph 5.9 the convergence of the maximum and minimum values of the two principal 
membrane forces N1 and N2 is illustrated. The full set of results can be found in Appendix A.2.1. 
The analysis results are displayed along with a dotted reference line, which is drawn from the last 
Robot result, i.e. of the results achieved with the finest mesh. This reference is later used for 
calculating the deviations of the Karamba results from the Robot results. In cases, where the Robot 
results do not converge, the deviations will also be greatly affected by the Robot inaccuracies.

The circumstances for convergence of the stresses are much more complex in this structure than 
for the continuous shell, where the forces are axis symmetric. One challenge is the adjustment of 
the mesh around the openings, particularly distinct in the peaks of the square mesh results, which 
is the consequence of having straight mesh lines, as introduced in Figure 5.1. Another challenge 
has to do with catching force concentrations. Whenever the number of elements is changed, the

placement of the nodes changes as well, which in an area of highly concentrated forces could lead 
to a significant change in forces with different meshes. This means that smooth convergence of 
concentrated forces is hard to come by, which is for example the case for the maximum values of 
membrane force N1 and N2, occurring at the narrow edges of the shell. At this point, it is important 
to note that the forces in Robot are given per node and in Karamba they are given per element, 
which automatically gives a slight difference in what forces are caught. The exact placement of 
the maximum membrane forces vary between the four areas marked with red in Figure 5.5a, from 
each mesh refinement. In contradiction, the minimum value of N1, which occurs at a continuous 
part of the shell, displays quick convergence, only with the exception of the square mesh, which 
has shown to be a bad fit for the non-continuous shell. The minimum values of N2, which occur 
at the edge of the small hole, are more inconsistent than minimum N1 at the continuous part of 
the shell, but show better convergence, than for the values occurring at the narrow part of the 
edge.  
 
(a) Areas of max. N1 and N2 
(b) Area of min. N1 
(c) Areas of min. N2 
Figure 5.5 ‒ Critical areas of principal membrane forces N1 and N2 
Considering the problems of the Laplacian mesh from the continuous shell, the fluctuations of the 
square mesh and the more consistent membrane forces of the Karamba mesh, the Karamba mesh 
is chosen as the preferred option. The lack of convergence in max N1 will be handled in the 
following section. 
In Table 5.8-Table 5.9 the deviations of the Karamba mesh results from the reference Robot results 
are displayed for respectively the principal membrane forces, principal moments and the max 
displacement. Only a selection of the results are included here, and the full set can be found in 
Appendix A.2.1. The deviations are both calculated as the difference between the two values and 
a percentage deviation; these will also be referred to as the absolute and the relative deviations.

Looking at the percentage deviation, the max. value of N2 is the worst fit to the Robot results, 
which is also quite clear from Graph 5.8. Here it is worth noticing that the Robot results do not 
seem to converge properly, meaning that this the challenge does not lie in Karamba alone. The 
relative deviation between the Karamba and Robot results and the lack of convergence are both 
exaggerated by the fact that the forces are very small. Apart from the mesh related issues of 
Karamba, the inaccuracies of the smaller forces and the slow convergence, could also be worsened 
by the programs approximating smaller values better than larger, which is a common issue for 
numerical methods such as the Finite Element Method. 
The numerically smaller forces, max N1 and N2 are both very inaccurate, and though they are on 
the safe side of the Robot result, possible deviations of 113% at a detailed mesh refinement and 
==PAGE:89==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
79 
 
non-consistent results do not enable very detailed design. The small forces are especially delicate 
because they verge into tension, which is undesirable in a reinforced concrete shell. The maximum 
compression forces are on the other hand of very high accuracy. 
In Graph 5.10 and Graph 5.11, the convergence of the extreme values of the principal moment 
M2 are displayed, these are followed by the selected deviations of the Karamba mesh moments.

Table 5.9 - Deviations of principal moments of Karamba mesh from Robot results 
The moment deviations are lower than those of the membrane forces are, but the results are still 
not extremely accurate. As it is suggested by the convergence plot of Graph 5.11, the Robot results 
have the worst convergence apart from the Square mesh. This suggests that the moments in this 
structure are generally hard to approximate. From the distribution of M2 in Figure 5.6, it can be 
seen that the minimum value occurs in a very limited area, i.e. there is a drastic change over a 
small distance. The problem cannot be tied directly to the simple Kirchoff-Love bending theory 
used in the Karamba elements, as the Robot elements are based on the slightly more complex 
Reissner-Mindlin theory, and still, the results do not converge properly. The max M2 values of 
Graph 5.10 all converge, but the Robot results settles at a value slightly higher than Karamba. 
==PAGE:91==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
81 
 
Generally, the differences in moments of Robot and Karamba, could be initiated by the difference 
in bending theory. The moments do not have a clear tendency between the magnitude of the force, 
and the accuracy level. Though deviations of 21% are much better than the 113% seen in the 
membrane forces, the deviations of the moments are large enough that they are not appropriate 
for detailed dimensioning. 
 
Figure 5.6 ‒ Distribution of principal moment M2 of ø50 shell

Figure 5.7 ‒ Deformations (x500) of ø50 shell 
The displacements of the shell are generally very small, in the case of pure self-weight, hence why 
the deformation in Figure 5.7 have been exaggerated with a factor of 500. The trouble with 
approximating small values cannot be seen from the displacements, as the max. displacements of 
Robot and the Karamba mesh are very similar. 
The maximum displacement occurs at the top edge of the largest hole, which seems reasonable, 
since this is where the shell is most poorly supported. From the deformation figure, it might be 
noticable that the narrow edges have a more drastic downwards rotation than the rest of the edge. 
This effect is clearer in the distribution of nodal rotations in Figure 5.8. The rotation of the narrow 
edge might also be the cause of the moment concentrations close to the edge seen in Figure 5.6. 
 
Figure 5.8 ‒ Distribution of rotational deformations in ø50 shell 
5.3.1.1 LOCAL MESH REFINEMENT 
In order to use the analysis results for comparison of different design options, it is desired to gain 
results from Karamba, which are more consistent. A way of improving the convergence is to 
enforce local mesh refinement in the problematic areas. Even without the knowledge that the 
previous analyses have provided, it seems highly intuitive to have a more refined mesh around 
the openings, than in the continuous parts of the shell.  
Max displacement 
==PAGE:93==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
83 
 
The Karamba mesh component already comes with the option of refining the mesh along the 
edges, but not parallel to the edges. In Figure 5.9 it can be seen that the elements in the critical 
areas of N1, are either connected to both edges or connected to an element on the other edge. 
This means that there is very little chance for the analysis to catch a change in the forces from 
one edge to the other, which is particularly critical due to a rapid change in the principal 
membrane force direction, a sketch of which is given in Figure 5.10.  
      
 
Figure 5.9 ‒ N1 distribution  of Karamba mesh with automatic mesh refinement 
 
(a) N1 
(b) N2  
 
Figure 5.10 ‒ Principal membrane force directions in critical area 
An extra mesh refinement is introduced by adding points to the mesh at a fixed distance from the 
edges of the holes and the bottom edge, an example of which is shown in Figure 5.11. This 
procedure does not only apply to this specific structure, as most shell structures would benefit 
from being more finely meshed close to the edge curves. 
 
==PAGE:94==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
84 
 
       
 
Figure 5.11 - N1 distribution  of Karamba mesh with manual mesh refinement around edges 
The stress analysis has been carried out on the mesh with the extra mesh refinement around the 
edges. The maximum values of N1 and N2 of the new mesh as illustrated alongside the original 
Karamba mesh and the Robot results in Graph 5.12 and Graph 5.13. It is relevant to note that the 
offset distance of the new points from the edge is constant throughout the analysis, and by having 
less variety in placement of the nodes and size of the elements, the results will automatically be 
more consistent. Controlling the elements in critical areas might eliminate the chance of randomly 
finding the exact extremes of the forces, but it increases the chance of finding good, consistent 
approximations, which is a considered to be a safer approach.  
 
Graph 5.12 - Max. principal membrane force N1 of Karamba mesh with edge refinement 
 
0,00
5,00
10,00
15,00
20,00
25,00
30,00
35,00
0
5000
10000
15000
20000
25000

[kN/m]
Number of elements
Karamba mesh
Karamba mesh ‐ Edge refinement

==PAGE:95==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
85 
 
 
Graph 5.13 - Max. principal membrane force N2 of Karamba mesh with edge refinement 
For both the N1 and N2, the results of the new mesh are much closer to the Robot results. 
Unfortunately, the new N2 results are slightly more uneven, but considering the size of the 
fluctuations, it is still considered an improvement. 
In Table 5.11-Table 5.13 a selection of the deviations of the new results from the Robot reference 
values of Table 5.8-Table 5.10, have been calculated, and the full set can be found in Appendix 
A.2.1. The problematic max N1 now have deviations in the area of 5-8%, which is significantly 
better than the previous 54%. The deviations of the min N2 has also greatly improved, though the 
relative deviations are still high, the absolute deviations of 0,9-1,1kN/m are of a similar size to the 
other results. A general accuracy level of the membrane forces cannot be given, as it is very much 
dependent on the placement and the magnitude of the force.

Table 5.12 - Deviations of principal moments of Karamba mesh with edge refinement from Robot results 
The deviations of the moments have not improved significantly, however the results are do show 
reasonable convergence as seen in Graph 5.14 and Graph 5.15. As the Robot results fluctuates at 
the end, Karamba is not necessarily to blame for the deviations. As Robot has been chosen as the 
reference, it must be assumed that inaccuracies in the order of 20% can be expected for the 
bending moments. The deviations could be caused by concentrated moments, and generally by a 
difference in the computational methods of the two programs. 
The maximum displacements of Table 5.13 are more or less unchanged with the new mesh, which 
is not surprising, as the mesh refinement is a local improvement, and has little effect on the global 
displacement field.

5.3.2 BUCKLING 
The buckling analysis of the ø50 shell has been carried out on the same model as the stress 
analysis. This means that this time the shell is simply supported, unlike the continuous shell, which 
was clamped at the edges.  
In Graph 5.16 the buckling loads of the Robot model and the four original mesh types are 
displayed, showing very rapid convergence. The full set of results can be found in Appendix A.2.2. 
The Laplacian mesh results have a slightly slower convergence than the others do, most likely due 
to the shape improvement, which happens with each mesh refinement. Also in this case, the 
Karamba mesh seems to have a slightly quicker and more even convergence than the others. 
 
Graph 5.16 - Buckling analysis of shell with openings 
In Table 5.14 the deviation of the buckling loads of the Karamba mesh model and the Robot model 
have been compared. Even at a very coarse mesh refinement the Karamba results are extremely 
similar to the Robot results. Since the deviations of the Robot buckling loads from the analytical 
solution in the case of the continuous shell were slightly higher, the previously found 3-5% 
deviations should be used as a guideline for the expected accuracy level in this type of analysis.

In Figure 5.12 the buckling modes of the ø50 shell, at a selection level of the mesh refinements 
are displayed. The deformations are very similar with significant bulging around the largest hole 
and slight deformations around the other holes. It can be seen that the shell switches between two 
different buckling modes, which apparently have very similar buckling loads. The buckling modes 
illustrate that the holes have a significant impact on the stiffness of the shell, substantiated by that 
fact that the buckling load was reduced from approximately 80kN/m2 to 20, from cutting holes 
and changing the supports from clamped to simple. 
 
 
 
 
Figure 5.12 ‒ Buckling modes of ø50 shell 
In order to check that the locally enforced mesh refinement does not compromise the overall 
performance of the structure, the buckling analysis is also carried out with the edge refined mesh, 
which is illustrated in Graph 5.17. The results are very similar to those of the other meshes, and 
even slightly closer to the robot results. It is especially interesting, that the accuracy of the results 
520 elements

are not compromised, when the main element size is increased in order maintain a constant 
number of elements as the edges are refined. 
 
Graph 5.17 - Buckling load of mesh with edge refinement 
5.3.3 HEAVY LOAD 
Up until this point, the shell has only been tested with an automatically generated load for self-
weight called Gravity. By running an analysis with the heaviest possible load combination, it is the 
aim to test both the load combination ability of Karamba and whether the structure performs 
differently under heavy load. In this case, only evenly distributed load is considered, but for 
dimensioning purposes it would be necessary to consider uneven live loads as well. The load case 
considered most critical is one with a dominating live load, as listed in Table 5.15. As the shell is 
a part of a public area, with the possibility of having larger events and gatherings a live load of 
5kN/m2 is considered. Additionally, the dead load has been increased with a load for the pavement 
on top of the concrete shell.

The analysis is only carried out with the manually refined Karamba mesh, as this has given the 
best results. In Graph 5.18 and Graph 5.19 the results of the problematic max. N1 and N2 are 
again displayed.  
 
Graph 5.18 ‒ Max. principal membrane force N1 of ø50 shell under heavy load 
 
Graph 5.19 - Max. principal membrane force N2 of ø50 shell under heavy load 
The graphs show that the Karamba results follow the same curve as with the pure self-weight, 
only at a different scale. This is to be expected, as all the forces are evenly distributed vertical 
loads. The Robot results have a similar but slightly different convergence compared to the self-
weight case. It is difficult to pin-point, the exact reason that the Robot results have not just been 
scaled, but since they converge towards the same results as Karamba, it is of little interest.

In Table 5.16-Table 5.18 the selection of deviations of membrane forces, moments and 
displacements have again been calculated, showing that the accuracy level of the heavily loaded 
structure is neither consistently better nor worse than that of the self-weight case in Table 5.8-
Table 5.10. This suggests that the size of the load has little influence on the accuracy of the results, 
when performing linear analysis, which was the assumption behind mainly testing the structures 
with self-weight. Also in this case, the full analysis can be found in Appendix A.2.3. In the case of 
non-linear stress analysis the story would have been a different one, since then the stiffness of the 
structure would be dependent on the load.

5.3.3.1 NON‐LINEAR DEFORMATIONS 
A large deformations analysis has been carried out on the heavily loaded shell, in order to discover, 
whether the incremental analysis can be used for investigating any non-linear deformations of the 
ø50 shell. 
Where the large deformations analysis in Karamba is a purely incremental non-linear analysis, the 
Robot analysis performs equilibrium iterations in between the increments, which gives a more 
exact approximation of the non-linear behaviour. Only deformations will be considered, as it has 
previously been shown that stresses cannot be obtained. 
In order to see the largest effect, the analysis has been carried out for the heavy load case. The 
analysis has been carried out on respectively the Karamba mesh with edge refinement and in 
Robot at the finest level of mesh refinement previously used, i.e. with approximately 23000 
elements. In this case, the variable for the analysis is the number of increments, as it was the case 
in the large deformations analysis of the methodical tests. In Table 5.19 the maximum 
displacement, occurring at the top of the largest hole, and the displacement in the center of the 
shell are listed for both Karamba and Robot. The increase defines the change in displacement from 
the linear case, and the deviation is the deviation of the Karamba results from the corresponding 
Robot result.

The displacements of Robot are unaffected by the number of increments, which is likely due to 
the iterations within each increment. The non-linear displacements of Karamba are significantly 
smaller than those from Robot are. In Figure 5.13 and Figure 5.14 the deformation field of Robot 
and Karamba are displayed with the deformations scaled up with a factor of 100. The Karamba 
==PAGE:105==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
95 
 
model show both the linear deformations in red and the non-linear deformations in green. The 
two figures show that the deformations are of a similar nature, but the Robot deformations are 
much larger. This leads to believe that the large deformations analysis in Karamba can be used to 
get an indication of geometrically non-linear behaviour, but the values of displacement should not 
be counted on. 
In the detailed deformation fields of Karamba in Figure 5.15, the wave effect around the holes are 
exaggerated with the non-linear analysis, such that the sections bending downwards are pushed 
downwards and the sections bending upwards are pushed upwards. The displacement values of 
the center of the shell in Table 5.19 shows that the non-linear deformations are much less distinct 
in the continuous part of the shell.

Figure 5.14 ‒ Linear (Red) and Non-linear (Green) deformations (x100) of Karamba 
==PAGE:106==
 
5  COMPARISON OF FINITE ELEMENT SOFTWARE 
96

==PAGE:107==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
97 
 
The two major aspects of determining the applicability of Karamba in the design process of a shell 
with holes are which analyses can be performed and what the accuracy levels of the analyses are. 
The analysis options available creates very clear limitations for the possibilities with Karamba, 
whereas the accuracies compared to a different FE program make for a much less definitive 
measure. As a basis for assessing the application of Karamba, a synopsis will be given of the results 
of the tested analysis types.  
Specific accuracy levels valid for all types of shell structures with openings cannot be stated from 
this limited set of analyses tailored to one specific design. However, a useful picture can be painted 
by summarising the accuracy levels achieved and the factors, which have shown to have a clear 
effect on the deviations. In further study these can then be the basis for improving the model and 
performing additional and more in-depth tests.  
 
Membrane forces. In a well-designed shell structure, the membrane forces will be much more 
prominent than the bending moments and therefore the main deciding factor for the cross section 
of the shell. The relative deviations of the membrane forces have been tied to the magnitude of 
the force relative to the overall force field. The extreme forces have been approximated with 2-3% 
deviations, whereas the smaller forces have not come consistently under 30%. The results did not 
show perfectly smooth convergence for all the membrane forces, which means that even when 
comparing very similar structures, the mesh refinement could induce inaccuracies. For the sake 
of approximate dimensioning, the extreme forces are the main interest, but for e.g. detailed 
reinforcement design, it is necessary to have good approximations to the entire force field. This 
means that Karamba has provided sufficiently accurate membrane forces for approximate 
dimensioning of the cross section, but no for the more detailed dimensioning. 
The main factor for the accuracy of the membrane forces has been the mesh refinement. This has 
been accommodated by implementing local mesh refinement in the problematic areas, since the 
computational time is highly sensitive towards the number of elements. The local mesh refinement 
is effective because of the force concentrations, which occur when cutting holes in a shell, 
especially when cutting holes close to the edge. 
Moments. When making decisions based on the stresses in the structure, the bending moments 
are inevitably tied to the membrane forces. However, the two are based on different methods of 
6 KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS
==PAGE:108==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
98 
 
calculations, and it therefore makes sense to evaluate them separately. An accuracy level of the 
bending analysis better than 20% deviations has not been made probable by the tests. The results 
showed minimal improvement when introducing the local mesh refinement, which could imply 
that the deviations are mainly caused by approximations in the computational method. As the 
Karamba moments came closer to the zero moments of the continuous shell than the Robot 
moments did, it seems that approximating moments in shell structures is generally a challenge.  
The moments are particularly interesting for reinforcement design, since in the areas where there 
is little to no compressional membrane forces, bending will cause tension in the cross section. Not 
only are the moments a deciding factor in where the shell should be strengthened, but the bending 
moments of a shell also have a strong connection to the displacements, and the moment 
distribution can therefore be a help in deciding where to stiffen the structure. 
Buckling. The linear buckling analysis has consistently been of very high accuracy, and deviations 
under 3% can realistically be achieved. The fact that the buckling analyses proved more consistent 
and accurate than the stress analyses could be an outcome of the approximate nature of the Finite 
Element Method, which entails that the global behaviour of a structure is easier to approximate 
than local phenomenons such as force concentrations.  
Unfortunately, the main restriction in applying the Karamba buckling analysis in the design 
process lies within the nature of linear buckling analysis and not the accuracy of the results. The 
linear buckling analysis should not be used for dimensioning of the shell, but rather as a design 
tool. The analysis can be used for testing the consequence of proposed design changes for the 
stiffness of the structure. As previously stated, the structures compared have to be of a similar 
nature, which in this case e.g. could mean that the structure has to be a positively curved shell of 
small height, in order to be comparable to the ø50 shell. Generally, it should be remembered that 
the more similar the structures are, the more comparable the linear buckling loads are. Decisions 
should also not be made based on small changes in buckling load. The safest way to conclude 
based on the linear buckling analysis is if the competing designs have the same buckling mode, 
but significantly different buckling loads. 
Buckling analysis is the subject in which there is the greatest gain in switching from Karamba to 
a heavier FE program. When the approximate geometry and openings are determined, it would be 
beneficial to perform a non-linear buckling analysis, in order to get a more realistic picture of the 
buckling load, and through that an idea of the relation between the linear and non-linear buckling 
behaviour. With this being said, it has also not been possible to perform a successful non-linear 
buckling analysis with shell elements in Robot, meaning that another FE program would have to 
be introduced. 
Deformations. The displacements of Karamba and Robot showed great similarities for both the 
continuous shell and the shell with openings but they deviated with 11,23% from the analytical 
==PAGE:109==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
99 
 
solution of the continuous shell. As previously mentioned, the displacements themselves will 
hardly ever be governing for shell structures, which is evident from the fact that the very shallow 
ø50 shell deflects only 15mm under heavy live load. For this reason, quick convergence and 
consistency with Robot are important factors for backing up the use of the displacements as a 
measure of the stiffness of the structure. The maximum displacement of Robot has been well 
approximated even at very coarse mesh refinement. However, looking at the displacements in a 
specific point, rather than only looking at maximum value, the variation of the results might have 
been higher.  
Non-linear deformation effects can be mimicked with the large deformations analysis, butthe 
values of displacement have not been found satisfying. This means that the non-linear analysis 
can be used to get an indication of whether distinctive non-linear deformations occur in certain 
areas of the shell and therefore a more complex non-linear analysis should be carried out. 
The deformation energy analysis was tested in order to gain a measure for the total deformations. 
The results showed very high accuracy, with under 1% deviations for the axial deformations and 
under 3% for the bending deformations. It does limit the weight of the assessment that the results 
have not been tested to more detailed, but solutions of 3d deformation energy are not easily 
accessible. As the analysis is mainly included as an example of how to further qualify the decision-
making process, the simple test have been deemed sufficient.   
6.1 ADVANTAGES  OF KARAMBA 
The main advantage of Karamba lies in the automatic adaption of the FE model, whenever a new 
surface geometry is provided. Once the Karamba script is set up in Grasshopper, when a surface 
is plugged in, the mesh, supports and loads are automatically adapted to the new geometry. This 
means that a variety of designs can be tested with very little effort. It should be said that some 
understanding of FE modelling and structures in general is necessary, in order to assess whether 
the FE model is applicable to the specific structure. 
With Karamba comes also other advantages, both in terms of the freedom, which Grasshopper 
provides and in terms of new options for analysis, some of which will be covered in the following. 
6.1.1 FLEXIBILITY OF PARAMETRIC FINITE ELEMENT MODEL 
Typically, FE programs include great options for the most common cases of supports, loads, cross 
sections etc. However, the options for customising these to more specific needs can be non-
existing or just very inflexible. This obviously only refers to what is possible within the user 
interface of a traditional FE program, because when coding is included, the options are greatly 
expanded, and are mostly limited by the abilities of the user. This is an area, where Karamba has 
==PAGE:110==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
100 
 
a large advantage, as it is incorporated into a parametric design tool, Grasshopper, which greatly 
improves the flexibility of the FE model. 
The tangential supports are a great example of an attribute, which is typically very difficult to 
execute in Finite Element programs. As mentioned for the spherical cap, rotational symmetry eases 
this process, but for other cases, the way to handle this problem in a conventional FE program, 
would probably be to model the supports for each individual node. In Grasshopper, there are great 
options for evaluating the surface geometry and this way set up a script, which customises the 
supports to the geometry at the edge. 
Another aspect, which is improved greatly with the flexibility of a parametric platform, is load 
modelling. Robot does have great options for simulating wind and snow compared to many other 
FE programs, but the options are generally hard to adapt to shell structures. Though rules of wind 
and snow behaviour have to be enforced manually in Grasshopper, the options for customising 
loads to irregular geometries are much greater. With the snow load as an example, a script can be 
set up, which evaluates the geometry of a mesh, and for each element the relation between the 
horizontal and the inclined area determines the magnitude of the snow load, and the inclination 
of the element determines how much of this snow slides of.  
The options for uneven live loads are even better, because these are much less bound by the rules 
of nature, which means that the possible variations are much harder to generalise. Applying 
irregular point or area loads to curved surfaces, and changing these, is eased by the parametric 
platform specialised for geometry. Another option, which Grasshopper facilitates, is optimisation. 
This could potentially be used in combination with Karamba for optimising a parametrised load, 
in order to find the worst case scenario. Even if the Karamba results were not found to be accurate 
enough for dimensioning, the load case found could then be modelled in Robot. The simplest case 
would be a point load, parametrised with respect to its x- and y-coordinates. An example of a 
service vehicle consisting of four point loads parametrised with respect to x- and y-coordinates 
and rotation is illustrated in Figure 6.1. 
 
==PAGE:111==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
101 
 
 
Figure 6.1 ‒ Parametrised service vehicle load 
The parametrisation is also a great help in designing non-uniform cross sections of the shell. Areas 
of different thicknesses, stiffnesses or reinforcement degrees can either be defined from 
geometrical considerations or based on forces and deformations found with Karamba. This does 
have its limitations in this case, as the accuracy level of the forces has not been found high enough 
for very detailed dimensioning of thickness and reinforcement. If the precision of the Karamba 
section forces was higher, there could even be a potential in using the principal stress directions 
and magnitudes to design an efficient reinforcement layout. 
6.1.2 DEFORMATION ENERGY 
Deformation energy is a tool, which would have little application in conventional FEA, but when 
incorporating FEA into a design process, any tool, which can help evaluate the quality of different 
design variations is helpful.  
As previously mentioned, the total deformation energy is a measurement for the overall 
deformations of the structure, and can be used to rate the efficiency of different structures. What 
is particularly relevant for shell structures is the relation between axial and bending deformation 
energy, i.e. how dominating the membrane behaviour is compared to the bending behaviour. In 
order to compare the deformation energy of different shells, they must be subjected to the same 
load, which means that if the shells are subjected to a uniform load of the same magnitude, the 
deformation energy can be divided with the surface area. 
Let us consider a fundamental concept of membrane theory: Shells must be thin for membrane 
theory to apply, because the thicker the shell becomes, the more dominating the bending 
behaviour becomes. This would mean that when testing the same shell with different thicknesses, 
that the percentage of bending deformation energy out of the total increases with the thickness. 
Another concept, which the deformation energy could help shed light over, is the change from the 
tangentially supported, continuous membrane shell to the simply supported ø50 shell with 
openings, which was made in the previous chapter.  
These two concepts are sought to be explored by looking at the deformation energy of varying 
thicknesses of respectively the continuous shell with tangential supports and pinned supports and 
==PAGE:112==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
102 
 
ultimately the ø50 shell with pinned supports, as shown in Table 6.1-Table 6.3. The analyses are 
carried out with the heavy load case.

From the three examples, the already familiar phenomenons can be detected along with some 
additional attributes:  
 
Increasing thickness leads to increasing relative bending deformations 
 
Replacing the tangential roller supports with pinned supports increases the relative 
bending deformations significantly, but slightly reduces the total energy 
 
The openings of the ø50 shell cause less increase in bending deformation energy the 
thicker the shell is 
 
At a certain thickness, the increase in thickness is no longer beneficial for the stiffness of 
the structure ‒ probably because the self-weight becomes governing 
The deformation energy can also help to determine the critical areas of the structure, as it is the 
total resulting strain of respectively the membrane forces and bending moments. In Figure 6.2 the 
distribution of the axial and bending deformation energy show where respectively the resulting 
membrane forces and bending moments are of highest impact. 
==PAGE:113==

6.2 APPLICATION 
In this section, an assessment will be made of the role, which Karamba could have at different 
stages of the design, based on the options and accuracies found through the tests. For this purpose 
the Levels of Development, LODs, introduced in Section 2.2.2 will be used as a framework. The 
ideal would be, to be able to drag a definite line in the design process, up until which Karamba 
would be able to cover all analyses. However, such a statement cannot be made based on this 
simple case of supports, loads, material behaviour etc. This would also prove a limitation, as even 
when Karamba does not cover all the necessary analyses, and a different FE program must be 
introduced, Karamba can still be a help in skipping unnecessary iterations back and forth between 
modelling software and Finite Element software. 
Formfinding and optimisation will not be included here, as this is not within the scope of the study, 
and these typically entail a different type of design process. It is worth remembering though that 
this was the type of design process, for which Karamba was originally created.  
 
LOD100. There is little need for performing structural analysis at LOD100, but if unfamiliar 
shapes are considered, Karamba can be used to gain an understanding of the forces. At this point 
in the design development, the overall structural concept has to be decided upon, and numbers of 
structural analysis will be of little relevance, as the conceptual decisions will mainly be made based 
on functionality and aesthetics. In some situations, it might be relevant to set up conceptual models 
to compare e.g. the amount of material, which different types of structures need to carry a certain 
load. Generally, caution should be taken if comparing fundamentally different structures purely 
on Finite Element results. 
==PAGE:114==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
104 
 
LOD200. Once the overall concept of the structure is decided upon, the options for comparing 
designs are much better. At this stage, Karamba can be a help in approximate dimensioning, but 
more substantially, in evaluating different geometries and support conditions, for example 
through: 
 
Section forces being used to approximate thickness, such that designs can be compared 
on material economy 
 
Buckling analysis being used for determining any major differences in the stiffness of the 
design options 
 
Deformation energy being used for comparing the deformations of designs under the 
same load 
Shape exploration is a great part of LOD200, and since the shapes of shell structures are very 
likely to be explored with the help of a parametric design tool, the road from surface model to FEA 
is very short with Karamba. 
LOD300. This is the level at which the shape is detailed and the primary cross section is 
dimensioned. The lack of non-linear buckling analysis does make it inadvisable to pass this Level 
of Development, without consulting another FE program, especially in situations where the linear 
buckling load is not greatly superior to the applied load. Karamba can however be a help in: 
 
Dimensioning of approximate cross sectional properties with respect to stress 
 
Using linear buckling analysis for testing stiffening initiatives 
 
Testing the impact of openings on forces and stiffness 
 
Testing the response of the structure to different kinds of uneven load 
LOD400. LOD400 is the level at which the design is completed for production. Karamba does not 
have its strength in extremely detailed design, both because of the linear buckling analysis, but 
also because the section forces have not proved sufficiently accurate. Where Karamba does have 
its strength, is in its flexibility, as described in Section 6.1, which means that the approximate 
results of Karamba can be used to ease the modelling in a heavier Finite Element program, e.g. by 
separating the surface into sections depending on the forces or determining the critical uneven 
loads. 
Documentation is also of great importance when finishing a design, which is definitely a strength 
of the more recognised FE programs, as they are typically able to auto-generate reports, with the 
information needed for approval.  
 
==PAGE:115==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
105 
 
6.2.1 CROSS SECTION 
A simple cross section calculation is set up in Grasshopper, in order to illustrate how the Karamba 
results can be used for estimating the dimensions and making design decisions.  
By defining the cross section within Grasshopper, it is possible to set up a material take off, which 
can be a big help in a decision-making process, as the material economy is often one of the main 
variables to consider.  
The calculations are simplified by assuming the cross section to have equal reinforcement in the 
top and the bottom, as illustrated in Figure 6.3, and furthermore the reinforcement is assumed 
isotropic, meaning that the reinforcement degree is the same in all cross sections of the shell. The 
simplified reinforcement is adequate for the detail level of dimensioning, which the section forces 
of Karamba have been deemed applicable to. 
The reinforcement will not be included in the actual Karamba analysis, which means that the forces 
are calculated based on the concrete stiffness alone. These results will then be used to dimension 
the reinforced cross section. 
 
Figure 6.3 - Shell cross section 
The stress distribution from the moments applied is an adaption of the sandwich model for 
reinforced concrete slabs, which has e.g. been describe by Nielsen (2011). This is a method, where 
the thickness of the compression zone of the concrete is chosen at a set value, t. 
The contribution from the membrane forces is found from Navier’s formula, with an equivalent 
concrete area, Ai. As the cross section is reinforced equally in top and bottom, the absolute values 
of the moments can be used, in order always to find the concrete stress in the compressed side of 
the shell and the steel stress in the tensile side. It is assumed that the concrete carries all 
compression, and the reinforcement carries all tension, which is an underestimation of the 
carrying capacity of the cross section. Since the membrane forces and moments do not work in 
the same direction, the contribution from the moment to the two principal membrane stresses, is 
always taken from the maximum moment in the respective point. This will overestimate the 
stresses in the shell, but for this approximate calculation, it is sufficient, as the alternative is 
extensive conversion of the forces. 
==PAGE:116==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
106 
 
From the stated assumptions, the stresses related to the two principal membrane forces of 
respectively the steel and the concrete are found as:

there is no contribution from neither in plane shear nor torsional moment. This leads to the simple 
criterion for the cross section that none of the stresses in the two directions can surpass the 
material strength, i.e. the utilisation must be less than 1:

As previously mentioned, the cross section machine can be used for creating a material take off. 
The concrete consumption can easily be found by multiplying the area of the shell surface with 
the thickness. The steel consumption will not be included here, because the calculations are 
complex and the results are not very telling for the structures, since the reinforcement amount 
can be greatly reduced by varying the reinforcement degree in correspondence with the stresses. 
6.2.2 DESIGN VARIATIONS 
Three types of design variations have been set up, in order to illustrate how the results of Karamba 
can help shed light over the impact of different types of design changes, and therefore qualify the 
decision-making process.   
This type of process, where form-finding is disregarded, obviously relies on human experience to 
suggest the design changes. Some of the suggestions presented are purely based on the expected 
behaviour of the ø50 shell, and some are results of the behaviour discovered through analysis of 
the structure. The variations will mainly be evaluated based on stresses, buckling load,

underlying factors such as economy, aesthetics and environmental impact would have to be 
weighted in order to make the design decisions. 
In order to make the thickness of the shell the only variable of the cross section, the same 
reinforcement size and distance and the same material properties will be used throughout the 
design studies, the values of which are listed in Table 6.4.

For dimensioning of the cross section, the thickness will only be varied in intervals of 5mm, as 
any less would be pointless in the scheme of the approximate cross section dimensioning. 
The stress analysis will be carried out with meshes of the finest level of mesh refinement tested, 
approximately 23000 elements, as smooth convergence was not achieved for all the section 
forces. The buckling analysis will be carried out with approximately 11000 elements. The meshes 
will vary slightly between the three cases in order to fit the specific design variations, which is 
why the results of the ø50 shell of 100mm might be slightly different to those determined 
previously. 
The shells are tested with the heavy load case, which is considered as the most critical, evenly 
distributed load. The total load is 12,4kN/m2 for the 100mm shell, but for the application on 
varying thicknesses, the individual loads are listed in Table 6.5.

6.2.2.1 HOLES BREAKING THE EDGE 
The design of the ø50 shell is based on an assumption that the openings should not break the 
bottom edge of the shell. This assumption was made based on the structural system, where the 
foundation carries all horizontal reactions as a ring in tension. As the foundation can be seen as 
an extension of the shell edge, the shell edge should be as wide as possible, to avoid large tensile 
stresses. 
It is sought to challenge this assumption, especially since in this study, the foundations are not 
included, and it is assumed that they can carry any horizontal loads. Through the previous 
==PAGE:118==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
108 
 
analyses, the narrow edge section has proved to be problematic, with large stress concentrations 
and large deformations. This could be a motivation for removing the narrow edge section.  
Two variations of the ø50 shell are tested and compared to the original, one where the two holes 
are moved 1m radially in the plan, such that they break the edge as seen in Figure 6.4(b); and one 
where the edge section is removed with plane sections as seen in Figure 6.4(c). The three examples 
will also be referred to as a, b and c accordingly. 
The decision of whether the holes should break the edge is considered to fit under LOD200, as it 
challenges the structural system of the shell. Since the two larger openings are essential to the 
concept of the structure, they are considered as a part of the main geometry of the shell.  
 
(a) Original ø50 shell 
(b) Shell with holes moved 0.5 m over the edge 
(c) Shell with edge section removed 
Figure 6.4 ‒ Design variations of holes breaking the edge 
As the first step in comparing the design variations, they have all been tested with a thickness of 
100mm. A selection of results of the three shells is given in Table 6.6, including the sum of the 
horizontal reactions around the edge, as an indication of the strain, which the shells put on the 
foundation.

First noticeable difference in the results of the three shells, is the increase of the minimum value 
of principal membrane force N2. For this reason, the distribution of N2 of the three shells is 
illustrated in Figure 6.5. The distribution of shell A and B are similar, but the distribution of shell 
C is clearly dominated by a concentrated force at the sharp corners. With the removal of the edge 
section, dominated by tension and bending, the maximum value of N2, which is compressive, has 
also increased. From this it seems that having a round hole instead of a straight helps to fade out 
the membrane forces. With a force as concentrated as the min. N2 of shell C, there is a high risk 
that the results are inaccurate, which should be tested in any further study of the shell. 
Shell C has a slightly higher axial deformation than A and B, which seems reasonable with the 
larger membrane forces. The fact that the maximum displacement of shell C is much larger than 
for A and B, but the overall deformation energy is only slightly heightened, could either be a sign 
of large, local deformations, or that the deformations cause little strain in the surface, but 
ultimately it shows that the total deformations of the three shells are not that different.    
The horizontal reactions are slightly smaller for shell C, in all likelihood due to the smaller surface 
area, which leads to smaller self-weight.

be reduced in thickness. In Table 6.7 the results of the shells with the new thicknesses are

Shell B does show slightly higher tension forces than the original, which would be the main reason, 
for not choosing this design option.   
The deformation energy of shell C is now the lowest, but only as a result of the greater thickness, 
which also means greater material consumption.  
With the behaviour of shell C, the Chris Williams quote of Section 3.3 gets an application 
(Adriaenssens, et al., 2014): 
Paradoxically, the less efficient the shell, in terms of shape, triangulations of the 
surface and boundary support, the better it behaves in buckling.   
Since the larger stresses in shell C necessitates a greater thickness than for shell A and B, the shell 
ends up with the largest buckling resistance, though this was not the case with the generic 
thickness of Table 6.6.  
In reality, buckling would probably be governing for the thickness all of the three shells, since a 
linear buckling load of 18-21kN/m2 cannot be considered safe compared to a maximum load of 
==PAGE:122==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
112 
 
approximately 12,4kN/m2. As none of the shells performs dramatically better in buckling in Table 
6.6, the choice is up to the designer. However, in terms of stresses, the shell type C does set the 
highest requirements for the material, whereas shell A and B perform equally well. In other words, 
with this model, there is no proof that it is better to have a continuous edge than not, but rounding 
of the holes does help to reduce the membrane forces.  
6.2.2.2 REMOVAL OF SMALL HOLE 
The smallest of the three holes in the shell can be considered as secondary and less necessary 
than the others are, as it does not provide entrance to the shell. Since the previous analyses have 
shown that some of the extreme section forces occur at the edge of the small hole, it is worth 
investigating how much the shell would benefit from not having the small hole. The two design 
variations here are the original ø50 shell and one where the small hole is removed, as illustrated 
in Figure 6.6.  
The effect of having the hole is tested, whilst the exact size and placement of the hole is 
disregarded. The addition of holes, which are secondary to the design, with approximate 
dimensions is considered a decision of LOD300.  
 
(a) Original ø50 shell 
(b) ø50 shell with small hole removed 
Figure 6.6 ‒ Design variations for removal of small hole 
In Table 6.8 a selection of the results of the two shell variations is listed, with the generic thickness 
of 100mm.

The membrane forces are affected by the removal of the small hole, but the most significant 
change lies in the moments, especially the positive moments, i.e. those, which cause upwards 
bending. In Figure 6.7 the distribution of the principal moment M1 is shown on the two shell 
variations. In the original shell, M1 is clearly most prominent around the small hole. With the 
removal of the hole, the values of M1 are reduced, and the less dominant bending behaviour 
becomes visible. This can also be seen in the reduction of the bending deformation energy. 
Taking away the small hole does improve the overall stiffness of the structure in terms of 
displacement and buckling resistance, but not significantly enough for that to be governing for 
which of the two design options to pick. This is not surprising as the maximum displacement and 
the main buckling deformations have previously been found to be concentrated around the largest 
opening.

Removing the hole can definitely help reduce the stresses in the shell, such that it could potentially 
be thinner. However, it again appears that the forces can be resisted with thin shells, which provide 
very little buckling resistance. This is also one of the characteristics and challenges of membrane 
shells, and it shows that even with all three holes, the shell carries transversal forces quite 
efficiently. 
As the linear buckling load is not remarkably improved by removing the hole, the motivation for 
doing so is very small.  
6.2.2.3 VARYING THICKNESS 
From the first analyses of the shell with openings, it was found that the two main problems of the 
shell were connected to the edges, one being the stress concentrations occurring at the narrow 
edge section, and the other being the buckling mode deforming the large opening. This has led to 
an examination of the benefits of increasing the thickness of the shell, respectively around the 
bottom edge and around the holes, in order to reduce the thickness in the remaining part of the 
shell. This also displays one of the benefits, of working within a parametric environment, as the 
shell can be divided into sections, of which the width and thickness can be varied.   
This type of design detailing would probably best be categorised under LOD350, as it is slightly 
more detailed than dimensioning the shell with one continuous thickness. Even more detail could 
be included, by differentiating the reinforcement between the sections.  
 
Karamba only permits one thickness per mesh, which means that a seperate mesh has to be 
provided for each thickness. The mesh sections are created by first meshing the complete surface 
with the extra edge refinement, such that the elements create rings around the edges. These 
element rings can then be separated into individual meshes. By using this approach, rather than 
modelling separate surfaces, it is guaranteed that the meshes will have coinciding nodes, and they 
will connect without any errors. 
For this study, the shell will be separated into three sections, which are illustrated in Figure 6.8, 
the bottom edge, marked with red, the edges of the holes, marked with green, and the main shell. 
The thickness in Karamba is separated equally on each side of the midsurface, whereas in reality 
it might be chosen only to increase the thickness downwards, however, the effect of this difference 
will not be considered here. 
==PAGE:126==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
116 
 
 
Figure 6.8 ‒ ø50 shell with edge zones of 0.3m width 
This is a variation with a number of variables, but for the initial examination of the 100mm shells, 
the two edge sections are given a width of 0,3m and are doubled in thickness. The thickening at 
the bottom and around the holes is tested separately and together, the results of which can be 
seen in Table 6.10.
