Table 6.10 ‒ Comparison of edge thickening with 0,3m width of edges and 200mm thickness 
==PAGE:127==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
117 
 
The increase in thickness of the two edge sections do improve the aspects which were intended 
of them: The thickening of the bottom edge reduces the tension stress in the reinforcement, and 
the thickening of the edges of the holes improve the buckling stiffness of the structure. However, 
thickening both edge sections at the same time seems to be an insignificant improvement from 
just thickening the edges of the holes. 
The increase in edge thickness also leads to increases in the forces. This is an effect, which comes 
with the thickness increase also being a stiffness increase, and the stiffer areas attract more forces. 
The axial stiffness has a linear increase from the thickness, but the bending stiffness has a cubic 
growth, which is why there is a drastic change in bending moments. The distribution of 
respectively N2 and M2 of the four examples are shown in Figure 6.9 and Figure 6.10.  
The distributions of N2 show that the principal membrane force, which was smoothly distributed 
around the openings in the uniform shell, becomes very concentrated when the edges around the 
holes are thickened. A similar behaviour can be seen from the distribution of M2, where the areas 
of minimum moment in the original ø50 shell are concentrated at respectively the bottom and the 
hole edge, when these are thickened. The distribution of both N2 and M2 are very similar for 
thickening of just the edges at holes and of all edges. This explains why increasing the thickness 
of the bottom edge does not reduce the stress, when the edges of the holes are already thickened, 
since the forces will concentrate around the holes. 
As it can be seen from the utilisation of the steel, the thickness around the holes cannot be 
increased indefinitely, because the stresses will become too high. However, this effect could be 
tackled by increasing the reinforcement degree in the thickened part of the shell. 
 
(a) Original ø50 shell 
(b) Bottom edge 0,3m ‒ double thickness 
==PAGE:128==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
118 
 
 
(c) Edges at holes 0,3m ‒ double thickness 
(d) All edges 0,3m ‒ double thickness 
Figure 6.9 ‒ Distribution of principal membrane force N2 of shells with thickened edges 
 
 
(a) Original ø50 shell 
(b) Bottom edge 0,3m ‒ double thickness 
 
(c) Edges at holes 0,3m ‒ double thickness 
(d) All edges 0,3m ‒ double thickness 
Figure 6.10 ‒ Distribution of principal moment M2of shells with thickened edges 
==PAGE:129==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
119 
 
As it has previously been shown, the stresses can be withstood with very little thickness, e.g. 
95mm for the original ø50 shell. It has not been possible to find good solutions to reducing the 
95mm from thickening the edges, when dimensioning with respect to stresses. Though it might 
seem redundant, thickening the edges has its greatest benefits, when the shell would otherwise 
have to be thick.  
As the buckling load of the 95mm ø50 shell of 17,8 kN/m2 is considered insufficient, it is chosen 
to set up a hypothetical scenario, where the thicknesses are dimensioned after a chosen buckling 
load. As it has previously been stated that the linear buckling analysis should not be used for 
dimensioning, this should only be seen as an example, which explores the benefits for the buckling 
resistance of the shell, from thickening the edges. It is chosen to dimension the shell after a linear 
buckling load, which is approximately four times the size of the heaviest load on the shell. With 
the heaviest load on the generic 100mm shell being 12,4kN/m2, a governing buckling load of 
50kN/m2 is chosen.   
Four variations, which live up to the 50kN/m2  buckling load are given in Table 6.11, one with a 
uniform thickness, one where both the bottom edge and the edges at the holes are thickened, and 
two where only the edges at the holes are stiffened with different widths. It is chosen to increase 
the width of the bottom edge section to 0,8m, to get a more noticeable benefit from the thickness 
increase.

Table 6.11 ‒ Comparison of shells with thickened edges dimensioned w.r.t. a hypothetical buckling load 
==PAGE:130==
 
6  KARAMBA IN THE DESIGN PROCESS 
120 
 
The variations show that there is little point in increasing the thickness of the bottom edge, when 
buckling is governing. The most efficient case is the shell where the edge of the holes of width 
0,6m are thickened, in which case, the material can be reduced with a significant 20%. This could 
possibly be improved by customising the thickness at each hole. With that being said, the thickness 
at the openings might be the thickness, which the shell is conceived to have, which means that 
the uniform shell could easily be chosen for aesthetical reasons. 
Generally, thickening the edges is a matter of finding a balance between the uniform shell, which 
carries the forces efficiently through membrane forces, and thick edges, which increase the 
buckling stiffness, but also the bending moments. 
The initial comparison of Table 6.10 did show a reduction of the stresses from increasing the 
thickness of the bottom edge, which means that in cases of more extreme stresses, this could be 
applicable. It is however clear that it is a choice of either thickening the bottom edge or the edges 
around the holes, since there is no benefit in doing both.  
 
==PAGE:131==
 
7  DISCUSSION 
121 
 
The abilities of Karamba as a tool for Finite Element Analysis of a shell structure with openings 
have been studied through a combination of tests of simple, analytical models and comparison 
with Robot Structural Analysis for the full shell structure.  
It was found that: 
 
The basic capabilities of calculating membrane forces, bending moments and linear 
buckling loads were validated in the analytical tests 
 
The meshing of the shell with openings was crucial for the accuracy of the membrane 
forces 
 
The membrane forces of Karamba were better approximated to the Robot results in the 
continuous parts of the shell than at the edges, and the deviations were inversely 
proportionate with the magnitude of the force 
 
The bending moments of Karamba were difficult to approximate to the Robot results 
 
The linear buckling analysis could be performed on coarse meshes in both Karamba and 
Robot with very similar results 
The method of assessing the quality of the results based on the results of another FE program 
does have its limitations. Any FE program will deviate somewhat from the theoretical solutions, 
which means that the deviations of Karamba from the theoretical solutions could potentially be 
both lower and higher than the deviations from the Robot results. There have also been analyses 
where the convergence of the Robot results has been worse than the convergence of the Karamba 
results, and the inaccuracies of Robot have then been transferred onto the deviations of Karamba. 
When no analytical model exist, the best numerical model must be chosen, whether Robot lives 
up to that, cannot be concluded from this study. The most thorough way to validate the results of 
Karamba, would be to compare it to a variety of different FE programs, but this would be too time 
consuming for this study.  
Through working with Robot, it has been found that when the element size becomes too small, the 
mesh is no longer refined evenly, and only the edges are subdivided. This could be the cause of 
the poor convergence of the Robot results in some of the analyses. One of the best ways to work 
around this, is to import a mesh from e.g. Grasshopper to Robot, instead of modelling the surface 
directly in Robot. However, this was not included in the study, as it was the intention to compare 
7 DISCUSSION
==PAGE:132==
 
7  DISCUSSION 
122 
 
Karamba to the pure Robot analysis, since Robot is often referred to as one of the better options 
for FEA of shell structures. This implies that there is generally a need for development in the field 
of Finite Element Analysis of shells. 
With this study, it was aimed to establish, whether the use of Karamba could be expanded from 
being a tool for conceptual design of optimal structures, to also apply to a more practical design 
process and more detailed design. This study provides no guarantee for how Karamba performs 
for different shell structures, but through the combination of methodical tests and analysis of the 
specific shell structure, it has been found justifiable to use Karamba for comparison of stresses, 
buckling stiffness and deformations, as long as no conclusions are made based on marginals. The 
comparison of designs should be made with a critical eye, as any major differences in behaviour 
could also entail major differences in the quality of the results. Additionally, Karamba can 
reasonably be used for approximate dimensioning with respect to stress, and with perfectioning 
of the mesh, the stresses could potentially be used for more detailed dimensioning. 
Karamba does not provide non-linear buckling analysis, and this will most likely be the deciding 
factor, in when to change to a different Finite Element program. As the relation between linear 
and non-linear buckling behaviour cannot be generalised, it takes a great amount of experience 
with buckling analysis to determine if buckling is critical for the structure based on the linear 
buckling analysis. The safer option is to always perform a non-linear buckling analysis, when 
dimensioning the final cross section of the shell.  
The full potential of Karamba is best illustrated as in Figure 7.1. Excluding the use of Karamba, 
when another Finite Element program is needed for non-linear buckling analysis or detailed stress 
analysis, will neglect the benefits of using Karamba for approximate analysis of irregular design 
changes, loads etc. before modelling them in the conventional Finite Element program. 
 
Figure 7.1 ‒ Potential application of Karamba in the design process of the ø50 shell 
==PAGE:133==
 
7  DISCUSSION 
123 
 
In the final chapter of the study, a number of design variations were analysed to exemplify the 
application of Karamba. One of the discoveries of the analyses was that thickening the edges 
around the openings was very beneficial for the buckling stiffness, and could lead to a significant 
reduction in the material consumption. By performing this design study within Grasshopper, the 
widths of the edge sections could be varied to find the most efficient solution. In many other FE 
programs, this would necessitate manual setup of a new model for each width change, which 
means that such a variation would be unlikely. 
7.1 FURTHER STUDIES 
A relatively simple model of the shell structure with openings has been used in this study, which 
means that much more study could be put in to how realistically the structure can be modelled. 
Four aspects, which would make great advances for the model and make the analysis more 
comprehensive, are foundation, imperfections, creep and shrinkage. Since the foundation will not 
be infinitely stiff, and it might even be provided with pre-tensioning, the deformation of the 
foundation could have a great impact on the forces in the shell. Creep and shrinkage are great 
challenges in in-situ cast concrete structures, particularly because the strains they cause, can lead 
to cracking of the concrete, which exposes the reinforcement. They cause strains in the shell, 
which could potentially be modelled as loads. Geometrical imperfections can have a great influence 
on the buckling stiffness, and with the right knowledge of the effects of the imperfections, these 
could easily be imposed on the surface geometry in Grasshopper. It is still relevant though, to 
consider the accuracies of the available results, when deciding how much detail to put into the 
model. 
As it was found that the mesh refinement at the edges had a great impact on the results of the 
membrane forces, these could potentially be improved even more, by perfect the mesh and making 
the meshing procedure more efficient. 
The next natural step in increasing the value of Karamba in a design process would be to improve 
the user interface, particularly the feedback of the analyses. Though not described in this report, 
initiatives were taken to ease the processing of the results and improve the understanding of the 
structure, such as collecting the relevant results in lists and mapping the results onto the shell, 
examples of which are shown in Figure 7.2. These examples could be advanced to help convey 
the consequences of a design change in terms of e.g. stresses and buckling stiffness, and provide 
insight into the behaviour of a structure. Making an educated assumption of which results are 
critical for the structure, and conveying them in an intuitive manner, could help improve a design 
discussion between experts and non-experts greatly. 
==PAGE:134==
 
7  DISCUSSION 
124 
 
 
(a) Selection of analysis results 
(b) Mapping of violated steel utilisation 
Figure 7.2 ‒ Examples of customised post-processing of results 
In this study the thickness, i.e. the concrete consumption has been the main variable for assessing 
design changes. A great increase in the value of the results could be achieved by converting them 
into numbers of material expenses, environmental imprint, construction expenses etc. With this 
being said, designing is job for humans and not computers, and many of the considerations in a 
design process cannot be quantified. 
==PAGE:135==
 
8  CONCLUSION 
125 
 
The objective of this thesis was to find a solution for performing structural analysis as an 
integrated part of a parametric design process. The existing Finite Element tool, Karamba, has 
been tested for this purpose on a case of a shell structure with large openings. From the studies, 
it has been validated that Karamba can be applied to the development of the design through: 
 
Exploration of the behaviour of the structure 
 
Comparison of designs of similar nature with respect to stresses, buckling stiffness and 
deformations 
 
Determination of approximate cross sectional dimensions with respect to stresses 
Karamba has not been found applicable to detailed dimensioning, and a more conventional Finite 
Element program must be introduced to complete the design.  
Karamba works within the parametric modelling tool Grasshopper, which means that structural 
analysis can be performed without a change in model format. By using Karamba, all design 
changes can be tested, and the Finite Element model will automatically adapt to the structure. This 
means that in the dialogue between the engineer and the architect, design changes can be tested 
as they are being discussed, which speeds up the decision-making process and brings an intuitive 
understanding of the consequence of the design change. 
This approach brings value to the design process by saving time, giving incentive to analyse more 
design variations and offering great flexibility for irregular analyses and design variations. Ideally, 
this can help improve the economy and the quality of the design. 
8 CONCLUSION
